HAVANA. May 19.â was
learned to -night on good au
thority at General Brooke's
headquarters that President McKin
i ley has strongly objected to the C.v- j
! bans surrendering their arms to the
I municipal authorities instead of to j
the United States Arsenal, and pend- j
; ing final decision General Brooke is
! withholding his decree ordering the j
' surrendering of arms and the distri- j
; bution of money. The delay is again
| causing dissatisfaction among the
''< Cubans, and if the order as agreed
upon between Gomez and' Brooke is
not carried out trouble seems to be
imminent.
Colonel Cespedes, speaking to-night |
for Gomez, said that General Gomez
made an agreement in good faith and
with every assurance from General
Brooke that the new order of things
would be carried out. If Brooke now
changes the order agreed upon he will
be breaking his word with Gomez and
the Cuban people, and Gomez will ab
solutely, refuse to enter into any more
negotiations with the United States
and will advise that the money be sent
home.
It is possible that if General Brooke
had insisted on Monday at his meeting
with Gomez that the Cubans should
surrender their arms at the arsenal
Gomez would have used his influence
to have this done. Now will persist*....
ently oppose it and every officer, and j
soldier will stand by him.
General Brooke states that he has re
ceived no Instructions from Washing
ton and will publish his decree to-mor
row. He would not say whether any
changes would be made. .
La Discusion says to-night: "Sec
retary Alger appears to wish to pro
voke a conflict here. He Is more dis
tinguished in the United States as a
business man than as a politician and
his relations with certain syndicates
are well known. He opposes everything
that Cuba wants and favors everything
that would cause bad feeling and pro
voke excitement. His attitude prompts
the question. What does he want? Does
he desire a war here similar to that in
the Philippines? We are forced to- be
lieve that he only approves what is un
satisfactory to Cuba."

MAYOR JAMES D. PHELAN
, has taken the first step toward
the erection of a statue of Ad-
miral- Dewey in this city, by
appointing a committee ' of
eighteen prominent citizens to take
charge of the movement and calling a
meeting of the committee for 2 o'clock
this afternoon at his office in the City
Hall. â¢.-./."
Mayor Phelan â¢is enthusiastic over
the proposition, and he knows the ne
cessity of acting in such a matter on
the instant. He realized that with such
short notice, some of the men he had
selected to act upon the comittee would
have previously arranged to spend Sat-
urday out of town, but he believed it
would be best to hold the initial meet-
ing at once, even though there were
some absentees, and get the matter
fully under way. His wisdom is shown
by a canvass of the gentlemen, for all
but three were communicated with last
evening, and of the fifteen only four
or five found it impossible to be in at-
tendance. Every One of them, though.
is. in full sympathy with the move-
ment and will 'be glad to serve on the
committee and work for the. success of
the enterprise. "' Â£i.--;
Although the Mayor only gave the
matter twenty-four hours' considera-
tion, he could not have been more for
tunate in his selection of committee
men had he taken it under advisement
for. a week. All are of , the high
est- standing In the city and State;
men of wealth who will aid the cause
liberally; of culture, who will take
pride in the plan and will see to it
that the monument! is a work of art
worthy of the city nad State and of
the man. and of such energy that once
starting in, they win carry the project
to rapid completion.
To hear the gentlemen selected talk
one would almost believe that their
task was already accomplished. There
was never a doubt as to the ultimate
success of their undertaking, and all
were eager to begin the work without
an instant's delay. There will be no
lagging when once they get together
and map out a plan of action. i
Not once has the question of funds
been raised. There is not a particle of
anxiety on that score. The one senti
ment that actuates the committee is
that the tribute; to the great admiral
must be appropriate. The members are
determined that San Francisco shall
erect a statue that- for imposing gran
deur and artistic, beauty shall be un
excelled anywhere.
This sentiment is merely in keeping
with the feeling that imbues' every citi
zen. One has only to read â  the letter
from J. P. Le Count given' below, 'â . or to
listen jto 'the expressions of every man
he meets to convince himself on that
point. Two days have passed since the
subject was first suggested and the
idea has spread to every portion of the
city and is as rapidly spreading
throughout : the State. At every point
j it is received with the same manifesta
! tions of approval. No man, woman or
â¢ child needs a .second to consider the
plan before giving it his loyal support.
In the. schools teachers used it as/ a
'. lesson in patriotism â lesson which
the ; pupils .loved to learn. "On the
streets, in the cars, everywhere the
Dewey statue was discussed and the
/first word of objection has yet to be
recorded... Men will not have to be
asked the second time to assist the pro
ject; few will need to be asked the first.
It may be only a quarter that can be
spared, but when the committee gets
down to work and calls for contribu
tions the possessor of that quarter will
go in search of an authorized collector
to deposit*!..
/ The Dewey monument will be erected
because there has never been a move
ment of the description that has ap
pealed so forcibly to every American
citizen as does this. .In no way could
the people express 'their gratitude and
approval of Dewey's achievement as in
building a monument that will speak
to this and to future generations of the
annihilation of a fleet that but for him
might have devastated these shores.'
Here are the sentiments of the gen
tlemen whom Mayor. Phelan has in
trusted'with the pleasing duty of car
rying out this' great work:

GENERAL SHAFTER
IN HEARTY ACCORD
â¢QAKEBSFIELD, May. 19.â Major General W. B. Shafter said to-
night: "I am heartily accord with the plan to erect said to-
night: "I am heartily in accord with the plan to erect a statue
. *â ' to Admiral George Dewey, commemorating his ; gallant and
brilliant victory at Manila. '< T v ':,\-'":'
The people of the State of California cannot do too much in
honor of the Acmiral of the American Navy. When his country
needed his services, both as a fighter and a diplomat, he was ready
and served with distinction in both capacities. Although his ships
were on the opposite side of the Pacific, the victories he gained
were of vital interest to the people of California. He either sunk
or captured the only Spanish ships which could attack this coast.
Golden Gate Park, overlooking the ocean in the waters of which he
fought, is an ideal place for a statue in his honor."

RINGS WITH A SOUND
OF TRUE PATRIOTISM
STEPHEN V. COSTELLO -I think
the suggestion of a monument to
Admiral Dewey is not only happy,
but timely. It rings to me. too,
with more of the true sound of patriot
ism than the Eastern voice now raised
in an endeavor to purchase a home for
the naval hero. That effort smacks too
much of commercialism to suit me. Oil
the, other hand . I have no doubt that the
movement suggested by The Call will be
hailed with delight and carried to speedy
success In a monument worthy of the
man and the deed it is to help perpet
uate.
SHOW APPRECIATION.
IN ENDURING BRONZE
BARCLAY HENLEYâ There is noth-
ing you can say sufficiently strong
to express my commendation of the
plan to erect a monument in Golden
Gate Park to Admiral Dewey. He Is a
wonderful manâ in my opinion, easily the
foremost naval commander in the world.
It is particularly appropriate, for reasons
so obvious that I shall not mention them,
that San Franciscans should j testify in
an enduring and magnificent monument
its appreciation of the hero of Manila
Bay.

The Nebraska Regiment returned to
Manila to-day for rest, having been on
the firing 'line since February 4. The
regiment participated in twenty-seven
engagements. including skirmishes, and
lost 225 killed and wounded.'
POPE LEO SEEKS
TO MAKE PEACE
LONDON, May 20.â A cable to the
Sun says: A dispatch from Rome to the
Daily News says that the Roman Cath
olic clergy in the Philippines recently
sent a memorandum to the Pope. ask
ing him to use his good offices at The
Hague to accommodate the dispute
with the United States. The Pope.
through the nuncios at Vienna.. Paris
and Madrid, asked whether the \ Aus
trian, French and Spanish govern
ments would undertake the matter in
his behalf. .Austria and France de
clined to dp so, on the ground that
nothing extraneous to the programme
could be discussed at the disarmament
conference.

McDONALD MAY
YET BE SAVED
Fighting Chance for the
Guard's Life.
â â â . â

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SpeciaJ Dispatch to The Call.
SAN QUENTIN PRISON, May 19.â
Guard James McDonald, the victim of
Convict Oppenheimer's knife, passed ah
easy night and is restful to-day. His con-
dition is largely due to the ministrations
of a trained nurse, who was secured at
the request of Prison Director .lames H.
Wilkins. It is now admitted that Mc-
Donald stands a fighting chance of re-
covery, due solely to his vitality and in-
domitable will power. Dr. Lawlor states,
however, that in case of blood-poisoning
, following traumatic pneumonia death
must ensue.
With the news of the guard's chance of j
life the excitement and indignation lead-
ing to a plan to lynch the murderer has
almost subsided. Sheriff Taylor Is pre- ;
pared' to cope, with any difficulty that may i
arise, and staled to-day that twenty-five !
, trusty men were in readiness to serve the
interests of law and order whenever it
became necessary.
The situation here is unchanged. Cap
â¢â¢ tain Edgar remains inflexible in his de
: termination not to place "Frisco Slim,"
i John Neill, "Jack" Wilson and William
: Phelan in the dungeon, and the guards
do not hesitate to say that the hatred
i borne Captain Birlem, who interfered in
the affair by request, is the reason for
I this apparent indifference. Not even the
fact that an unruly convict was found in
. possession of a dirk last evening has pro-
duced any change in Edgar's or Hale's
policy.
"If any of my men are afraid." says Mr.
Hale, "let them resign and I will fill their
places with men who are not."
The guards assert that this stricture is
unjust. They are not afraid, but think
It the better part of valor to take pre-
cautions against being stabbed in the
back when there is danger of it. At least,
so the guards say, the suspects should be
searched every day for weapons.
One piece of information hitherto print-
ed is untrue. It was stated that the con-
vict found with a knife yesterday was
thrown into the dungeon. Warden Hale
; and Captain Edgar have not even seen
| fit as yet to punish the prisoner, for it
. ! would be a tacit admission that theirs is
! the. policy of error.
It is stated on good authority that the
' existing condition of affairs will be drawn
i to the attention of the Prison Directors
by a petition to be presented at their
| meeting at Folsom to-morrow. Director
Wilkins has been appealed to personally,
but refuses to make any statement for
publication. .:':-
Guard McDonald took a sudden turn
for the worse this evening, and the offi
cials believe his death is only a question
of hours.

PROFESSOR PERHAM WELCOME.
Important Educational Work for Him
at Stockton.
STOCKTON, May 19â The action of the
Board of Education last night in select
ing Professor F. E. Perham to be the
head of the local High School gives gen
eral satisfaction. Professor Perham is. at
present Superintendent of Schools of
Berkeley. D. R. Wood will also come
from the University of California to take
charge of the science classes. Professor
D. A. Moberly. who resigned the princi
pal-hip of the. High School, will, it is un
derstood, enter the Presbyterian ministry.
Professor Perham comes at a time when
he will have his hands full. A new High
School building is in contemplation and
there is a desire on the part of many to
rearrange the system of study in the
higher classes with a view of giving the
school a higher rating abroad. Professor
Perham will have a salary equal to any
paid in the State in similar positions. He
was not an applicant for the position, but
the board has had him in view for some
time. :Y~-:~Y
SURVEY OF CANAL ROUTES.
WASHINGTON, May 19.-Amon^ those
under consideration for membership on
the new isthmian Canal Commission as
engineers from civil life are Messrs. Bogue
of New York, formerly chief engineer of
the Union Pacific Railroad; Morrison of
New York, a prominent bridge engineer,
and Noble of Chicago, at present a mem
ber of the Deep Waterway Commission.
As the rainy season is about to begin on
the isthmus it is realized that- the com
mission cannot do much in the way of
active operations at present beyond mak
ing a trip over the Panama, route via the
railroad and perhaps making some inves
tigations into the comparative merits of
the different ports that must form the
termini of the canal. In the early fall,
however, operations will be prosecuted
with the utmost vigor and an attempt
made to carry out the. direction of Con
gress to submit a report during the next
session. .

DICK O'BRIEN IS
DEFEATED BY WALCOTT
NEW YORK, May 19.â Walcott, the
colored boxer, who has deserted the ranks
of the lightweights to become a middle
j weight, knocked out Dick. O'Brien in the
1 fourteenth round of their fight at the
: Broadway Athletic Club to-night. Wal
: colt made a heavy concession of weight
; to the Lewiston man, yet he won without
! having to take more than, a half dozen
punches in return for the scores that he
! rained upon his man. Walcott took a lead
with the sound of the gong and, save for
a few seconds in the thirteenth, was
never, headed. He showed superiority, in
every respect and it was only O'Brien's
wonderful stamina and capacity to take
punishment that saved him from an ear-
lier defeat. Half a dozen times during
the fight and before the final, round he
seemed to be going, but every time his
strength came back and the rests revived
him. It was a fierce tight from the start,
yet it was so uneven that it failed to stir
the crowd to a very high pitch of enthu
siasm. Walcott showed improved form at
the increased weight and was wonderfully
clever.
SALE OF A COPPER MINE.
STOCKTON, May 19.â deal has just
been completed in this city by which J. A.
Fersom disposed of the Ne Plus Ultra
copper mine, nine miles from Madera, for
$125,000, the purchasers being New York
capitalists. Dr. Cross of this city was
also heavily interested. Copper is now
worth 19 cents a pound and considerable
copper mining property in this part of the
country has lately been disposed of at
good- figures. The mines at Copperopolis,
which have been closed for many years,
will probably be opened within a few
weeks. Active work is also to be resumed
in the mines near Campo in Cala
veras County, and prospectors are out
in the hills seeking new -veins of copper
ore. The company which bought the mine'
in Madera will put up a $150,000 smeller
and will commence work at once. .

AWFUL FATE OF A
MARINE BLASPHEMER
"I Will Euchre God Almighty's
Storm,,, Swore the Captain,
aod His Ship Became a
Floatiog CoffiQ.
VANCOUVER, 8.C., May 19.â Dr. H. Martin Doyle of New Castle tells
a thrilling tale of the awful fate of a marine blasphemer. The bark Brough
ton started some time ago from New Castle to Coquimbo. Before starting
the cook and steward were taken sick with typhoid fever. The doctor or
dered them to a hospital. ; The captain refused to let them go. The Port
Health Officer was summoned and told the captain that the men would con
taminate the food, and the ship would be a floating coffin in a month, and at
any rate helpless in a storm. The captain pushed the health officer off the
gangplank and made for sea, shouting: "I'll euchre God Almighty's storm
and bluff the eternal sea if every man dies on board."
A letter from the carpenter of the ship to the doctor tells the rest:
Three weeks out we struck a circular cyclone. I was sick in bed for three days,
and turned out to find the ship tearing around in a wide circle at an awful speed.
There was no one at the wheel. I went fro the captain's cabin. He was dead. I
went: to the first mate's cabin and the second mate's cabin. The first mate was dead,
the second mate dying. I rushed below and the cook and steward were huddled in
the bunks lifeless,' all dead of typhoid fever. Myself and three others were alone
left. The third mate could not navigate the ship, and I was too sick, and for twenty
seven 'days we chased around in the awful maelstrom until the cyclone got tired and
turned Into a three weeks' hurricane. At last, battered, dismasted, a floating dere
lict, we were taken' in tow by a passing steamship.
The crew blame it all on the captain, saying he could not bluff the Al
mighty.
PEOPLE WANT
PURE WATER
Â»
Sacramento Trustees
Sacramento Trustees
Face the Problem.
â â¦ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
'''.''' Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, May 19.â citi
zens of Sacramento. are paying consid
erable attention at this time to the
subject of pure water. The Chamber
of Commerce Iras called upon the Board
of Trustees for the appointment of a
commission of twenty-five citizens to
investigate the several water supply
propositions now before the city and
report which one, in its opinion', is the
best to adopt. Opposition to this ac
tion has developed - in the Board of
Trustees and the board has taken an
adjournment until next Monday night,
when it will say whether or not it will
act upon the suggestion of the Chamber
of Commerce.
Sacramento draws its water supply
from the Sacramento River, which
courses along its western edge. The
fastidious have time and again found
objection to the presence of mud in the
water, while others have gone so far
as to enter a mild protest against
drinking water from a river down
which dead horses and other animals
have a. habit of floating.' That Sacra-
mento needs some kind of a new source
of supply is conceded by the progressive
element, and, in fact, by all except
those who imagine that nothing can be
the matter with water that they have
drank for many years and yet lived.
Following out the desire to hit upon
some desirable plan the city recently
' voted $1500 for the purpose of enabling
an Eastern filtration company to make
experiments with clarifying the Sacra-
mento River water and freeing it from
objectionable germs. The tests were
conducted near the water works with
more or less success, but the people
have not thus far evinced much en-
thusiasm for this method.
Another plan exploited at consider-
length by the Chamber of Com-
merce is the securing of water from ar
tesian wells east of the city. Pumps
have been put at work to lift the water
from deep wells and a good supply of
clear water has been obtained.
Another proposition that has been
made to the city comes from the North
Fork Ditch Company, which offers to
pipe water in thirty-six-inch mains
from its works near Auburn. The water
is to run to a point opposite Folsom,
about 300 feet above the level of Sac-
ramento, where it is to be. filtered by
the gravity process and then turned
into a thirty-six-inch main, which is to
carry the water to this city, over twenty
i miles distant. It is claimed by this
. company that should the city adopt this
[ plan there will be no further use for
fire engines,* oiving to the tremendous
force which 'the 300-foot fall will give
the water.
Meanwhile timid citizens who do not
like to take "Sacramento straight" are
securing, in gallon quantities, water
j pumped from a locality southeast of the
city bearing the cheerful name of
j "Whisky Hill. Between drinks they are
| wondering whether the city authorities
are going to give them a regular sup-
ply of clear, pure water, or whether
they, will have to overcome their
scruples and drink from the river, as
of old. - â  -

BREACH OF PROMISE
DEMURRER SUSTAINED
SANTA BARBARA. May 19.â Con
nell-Douglas breach of promise case was
again before the Superior Court to-day.
It came up on demurrer. Two weeks ago
the plaintiff had been given permission to
amend his complaint in order to bring out
more fully the time at which. Louisa
Keutzler gave him her promise of mar
riage, and this amended complaint was
subject to a demurrer, which was ar
gued to-day. It sets forth that the cause
of the action is barred by the statute of
limitation, which provides that a breach
of promise suit must be commenced with
in two years after the promise is broken.
The amended complaint states that the
promise to marry was given in July.
1896, but it was not, to the satisfaction of
the defense, stated when the engagement
was declared off. The plaintiff's attor
ney said that the complaint stated con
cisely that the engagement was broken
off when Miss Keutzler married Mr.
Douglas in August, 1897. thus coming
within the two-year limitation. The de
fense held that the complaint contained
allegations that the defendant had pre
vious to the Douglas marriage pressed
his claim and had been as frequently re
jected by the now Mrs. Douglas, and that
the promise was therefore broken before
the marriage and more than two years
before the action was commenced. The
court sustained the demurrer and the
plaintiff was given ten days in which to
establish more clearly the date upon
which the engagement was broken.
SAM ERTEL'S CRIME.
STOCKTON, May 19.â Sam Ertel was
arrested by Sheriff Sibley early this
morning just east of town on suspicion
that he was the man who last night at
tempted to assassinate Clarence L.
Thompson while the latter was sitting
in his home at Holden.
The suspicions of the officers were
aroused when they noticed Ertel's appar
ent uneasiness as they approached him.
His own agitation. gave him away. At
the jail he said:
"Yes, I did shoot Thompson. If it had
been a little bit lighter I would have
killed the â , too. Thompson
swindled me," he continued. "We were
partners together in fourteen acres of
land out there and he did me up."
Some time ago Ertel sold his share to
another, but there has been bad blood be
tween the men ever since their copartner
ship. â ' -:
After firing the three shots at Thomp
son in the dark and then fleeing. Ertel
headed for this -city. He slept in a hay
cock last night and was making his wav
here after his night's rest when cap
tured. On his person was the pistol
which did the work. It had all six cham
bers loaded and Ertel had several extra
cartridges in his pocket.
DREYFUS UNFAIRLY TREATED.
NEW YORK, May 19.â A cable to the
Sun from Paris says: The .Figaro this
morning reviews the proceedings of the
court-martial which condemned Dreyfus,
who it says 'was not confronted by even
a single witness out of the twenty-three
who testified. All of these were military
men except Cochefprt. The paper recalls
the various incidents of the trial, show
ing that Dreyfus was unfairly treated.
It recalls the effect of this evidence on
Captain Freiestaette, who declared after
hearing Henry's evidence, "My conviction
is formed." The paper says there was
probably never a verdict returned on
flimsier evidence than that given in this
extraordinary case.
SUICIDE OF A CAPITALIST.
SAN JOSE, May 19.â William A. Senter, I
a capitalist, committed suicide this even
ing by blowing out his brains with a pis- |
tol, the act having been committed at his ;
home while standing in front of a look- ,
ing glass. He had no domestic troubles â 
or financial worries, but was a heavy j
drinker. He was intoxicated when he i
committed the act. He was 66 years of
age and a native of Maine. A widow and
six children survive him.
Sugar Beets in New England.
GROVELAND, Mass., May 19.â A plan
is on foot here to form a company to be
called the New England Beet Sugar Com
pany, with the object of building a mil
lion dollar factory for converting sugar
beets into sugar. It is hoped to persuade
farmers in New England. to cultivate
sugar beets. Seed and $4 a ton for beets
raised will be offered to the farmers.

DISASTROUS FIRE
VISITS JEROME
Three Hundred Build-
ings Destroyed.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
JEROME, Ariz.. May 19.â The third dis
astrous fire to visit Jerome within the
past eighteen months swept a large part
of the business portion of this town at
10 o'clock to-day. The fire had its ori
gin in the office of .the Leland Hotel,
which was a twenty-room, two-story
frame building. It spread rapidly until
three . hundred buildings had been de
stroyed. . â¢".",-"â â¢.
From the Leland Hotel the fire spread
over two squares, destroying the Ryan
Hotel, Scott & Moore's livery stable and
ten dwellings, stopping within ten feet
of the Reporter office. "West of the Le
land is a business section where a uarg.
number of hotels, restaurants and sa
loons were located. The St. Elmo, Elite,
Arizona, Miners' Exchange,. Fritz's
Place, Fashion, Annex, Red Light, Sen
ate, Connor's, Neef & Mattox, all saloons,
were destroyed within ' a space of
twenty-five minutes.
The estimated value of the destroyed
buildings is $45,000. Twelve Chinese res
taurants and at least seventy-five small
buildings in what is, known as the Mexi
can and Chinese quarters were consumed.
The Are alarm was given by the big
whistles at the United Verde Copper
Company's smelter, and 500 men were sent
down the hill by this company to fight
the flames. The new four-story brick
building belonging to the T. F. Miller
Company was saved with slight damage.
The Hotel Connor, a two-story brick
completed within the fortnight, was com
pletely gutted; the cost of the building
was $.7,-00, insurance $14,500.
Dr. E. T. Cody, whose office was in the
Leland, was the first to hear the cry of
a child in the office of the hotel, and has
tened there and found a fire started on
a bed. He attempted to smother the
liames with bed clothing, and was pain
fully burned about the head and face,
but no serious results are expected. This
is the only serious accident known at
this time. : ' /. "
Larkin's large clothing store was badly
damaged; insurance light. Hoover & Cor
diner, proprietors of the Fashion saloon,
lost their property at the September fire
and were replacing it with what was con
sidered to be a fireproof building at a
cost of $17,000. The walls of this build
ing were badly cracked and its contents
destroyed; no insurance.
The owner of the Leland, Mrs. M.
Smith, Is of the opinion that the Are was
of incendiary origin, and arrests will be
made of suspected persons. "â¢
The individual losers so far as known
are as follows: The New York stor.e in
the Kuchler brick block, made almost a
total loss from smoke and water, the
losses being estimated at $10,000; T6vrea
& Clay, small loss; Lyons & Kooblock,
nominal; Scott bowling alley, $800; C. M.
Clark & Son, notions, $2800; Parke &
Lewis, barbers, $800; Neff & Maddox,
$2000; Annex saloOn, $2000; Senate saloon,
$1500; Hoover & Cordiner, $.000 on furni
ture and new building, $2000 on saloon;
David Connor, $5000; J. C. Lanham, Elite
saloon, stock and fixtures. $3000: Dora E.
Clark, building, etc., $2500; Hooker &
McFarland, St. Elmo saloon, building,
stock and fixtures, $3000; Jose Tam
borino, Arizona saloon, $1500; Red Light
saloon, E. Carroll, $800; Mclntyre & Co.,
$600; Fritz Williams, $500; Allen Johnson,
blacksmith. $700; B. Qulnjado, $400; L.

JAPANESE ON TRIAL.
The Plantation Rioters of Hawaii Are
Haled Into Court.
VICTORIA, May 19.â Miowera
brings advices from Honolulu that the
Japanese who were concerned in the big
plantation riots in which a number of
Chinese were killed are now on trial. A
jury was inpaneled shortly before the
Miowera sailed.
A new bank to be called the First Amer
ican Bank has been established. Stock
of $1,000,900 has been over-subscribed.
Legislation has been passed whereby
only one-half the labor on plantations be
performed by Japanese.'

Â«Â«_ Kearny St., San Francisco. CaLPEACE DREAM
OF NICHOLAS
His Programme the Fed-
eration of All Nations
of the World.
LONDON, May 19. â The Westminster Gazette gives a
forecast of the Czar's peace programme, which for-
mulates an idea, practically, of the federation of the
world, with the disarmament conference as the starting
point. The conference is to become a permanent tribunal,
meeting every three or five years.
It also is proposed to establish a small body to settle
questions of fact, reference of which to the tribunal will not
be obligatory. The decisions of the minor body will not
necessarily be accepted, but it is expected that its conve
nience will make the practice universal of submitting ques
tions of honor to the mediation suggested.
lii regard to armament it is proposed to arrest their in
crease and to proportion military and naval estimates' to the
total expenditure.
Professor yon Stengel of Munich University, one of
Germany's delegates to the Peace Conference, says he now
regrets having: issued his pamphlets defending war and de
claring that eternal peace ought not to be the/ aim of cul
ture.

STOCKTON AGITATED
CONCERNING POLITICS
Democrats After Patronage, While
Republicans Read the Independ-
Republicans Read the Independ-
â¢/ ent Out of the Party.
STOCKTON, May 19.-Now- that the
municipal election is over the Democrats
are disagreeing between themselves as to
how the pap shall be divided. As the re
sults of the election remove any possibil
ity of this kind of trouble among Repub
licans, they are now trying to hunt out
the members of the party who went over
to the enemy and brought defeat To the
ticket.
The Republican city committee is angry
and last evening adopted resolutions sar
castic in vein, and virtually read the In
dependent out of the party for its policy
during the campaign.
Some of the "Whereases" read as fol
lows: ' ---'-. -
Whereas. This committee has felt, and does
: now feel, that It should have had the hearty
support of papers claiming to be Republican,
one of which has for years thrived and wax. 1
fat on the results of Republican success; and
Whereas, This committee feels that in the
matter â¢>.' journalistic support it can recognize
the difference between osininity and fearless
ness, between impotent, meaningless reference
to party Interests and their vigorous advocacy;
between that which Is neither cold nor warm,
and that spirit which places a paper In , har
mony with those striving for the best Interests
of the entire tarty, rather than any particular
clique; and
Whereas, We believe these facts should be
considered and hereafter remembered by the
party and It. successful nominees when dis
tributing the legitimate fruits of victory.
The "resolves" compliment the Daily
Record for Its able fight and loyal sup
port.

â  Whlttier Reform School Governor Henry I

Smith continue to make a neck and neck |
race. Behind Sherman Smith is United |
States District Attorney Frank P. Flint.
who has informed the Governor that this |
appointment is the 'single favor he has to I
ask of the State administration.

nearly all the. members of the medical

thropic Interest in the Whlttier School. j

publican State machine promised that he

situation has complicated the Whittier !
superintendence* so that it Is to-day tied j .

made an urgent plea for Sherman Smith.

nor is reported to have said in reply to
Flint's request. Hervey Lindley is said
to be taking a hand in the situation In
opposition to Dr. Barber.

day. Friday â¢ and Saturday,

that every county in the district, which
includes Butte, Colusa, Glenn, Tehama,

represented, both as to \acing stock
and the enthusiast in the sport. The
track will be in splendid condition on

OBSERVANCE OF
THE SABBATH
Discussion Before the
Presbyterians.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MINNEAPOLIS. Minn., May 19.â
Sunday observance was the question
which took the major part of the Pres
byterian Assembly's time to-day. Most
of the long afternoon session was de
voted to an earnest discussion of the
report of the special committee on Sab
bath observance. In the end the com
mittee's eight resolutions were adopted
with slight amendments, and two more
were added.
In the discussion the Sunday street
car, the Sunday train and railroad ex
cursion, the Sunday newspaper, the
Sunday working Presbyterian and
every form of activity which is not in
harmony with the literal meaning of
the fourth commandment was con
demned. Y'Y'Y'
Another special committee reported
that it had not been able to keep Con
gress and the President from granting
the Roman Catholic church permission
to erect a chapel on the West Point
Military Academy reservation.
â¢ In the debate on the Sabbath resolu
tions almost every* speaker gave evi
dence of being imbued with the feeling
expressed in the words of the commit
tee: "The American Christian Sabbath
is in imminent peril; in fact, in many
of our large cities and in other parts
of our land it is already nearly lost.
This means that the American liberty
and American institutions are. in peril,
for of these the American Sabbath has
been both the foundation and the pro
tection."
Rev. Frederick A. Doane of Califor
nia testified that he never, rode in a
street car on Sunday and that he
stopped over Sunday on his way to fhe
assembly.
D. XV. Glass of Baltimore deplored
Sabbath violation by Presbyterians,
and remarked that President Cowen of
the Baltimore and Ohio Railroad,
which discriminates in favor of Sun
day travel, Is a Presbyterian. On the
other hand, it was pointed out that the
many downtown churches would have
to be closed if church members never
rode to church. /-/-â¢
As adopted the amendments depre
cate the secularizing of the Sabbath.
urges members and young people of the
church to realize the importance of
Sabbath observation; legislatures and
Congress are urged to protect > the
American Sabbath; the American Sab
bath Union and the Women's; National
Sabbath Alliance are commended;
every pastor is recommended to preach
on Sabbath observance on some Sab
bath of next October and take a col
lection for the Sabbath Union; the
board of publication is requested to
prevail on Sabbath-school superintend
ents to use a specific Sabbath observ
ance leaflet; sympathy is expressed
with three million Americans who, be
cause of Sabbath desecration, are com
pelled to do secular work on the Sab
bath. \ ' . .'â¢ /
President McKinley-'s attention is re
spectfully called to the fact that em
ployes of the Postoffice and many other
departments of the Government are
compelled to work on Sunday.
The McGlffert case, contrary to ex
pectations, was not reached to-day. It
is likely to 'come up through the read
ing of Dr. McGiffert's letter to-morrow
morfcing. â¢ . .-
To-night the commissioners attended
a great public Sabbath observation
meeting In Westminster Church.
The moderator of the General Assem
bly has issued the following appeal to
all Presbyterian churches in the United
States:
Inasmuch as all boards' of our church
have closed the year without any Indebt
edness save the' freedmen's board, I,
Robert F. Sample, moderator of the, Ge
neral Assembly, in session at Minneapo
lis. Minn., do hereby appeal to all Pres
byterian churches throughout â¢ the United
States to make offering for this, cause on
the Sabbath. May 21. and report the same
to me by telegram Monday morning. This
appeal is made that for the first time in
twenty-five years our assembly may con
clude its sessions free from the burden
of debt, which amounts to $40,000.
F. SAMPLE, Moderator.

Special Dispatch to Tha GalL
Special Dispatch .to The Call.
NEW YORK, May 19.â The Herald is
informed on most, excellent authority
that proof tending to establish the fol
lowing startling statement will soon be
forthcoming:
That Police Captain John M. O'Keefe.
who died on November ,'5 last from in
juries said to have been caused by fall
ing downstairs, was the victim of a
most cowardly and brutal murder:
that the object of the murder was the
sealing of his lips because he had ob
tained information *)f a most infamous
plot which had for its object the as
sassination of Superintendent of Elec
tions John McCullagh,. former Chief of
Police, on November 6 â election
day; that when the details of the plot
are made ~ublic they will involve sev
eral of the best known Democratic pol
iticians in New York. ;
The intent of .'those, who originated
the plot, according to the statements
made, was to cause an assassination
similar in many ways. to the killing of
Robert Ross at Troy, New York, on
March 6, 15.6, by the notorious "Bat"
Shea, a political heeler. who. it was
said, had planned with certain , poli
ticians to kill Ross and his brother
William because of their activity at the
polls in their attempts to prevent the
operation of repeaters'- who had cone to
Troy from New York. Albany and
Syracuse to carry the election for. those
who em-Moved them. â¢
"Bat" Shea was tried and found guil
ty of the murder of Robert Ross, and
on February 11. 1897, was- executed in
Sing Sing prison.
The new facts which throw light
upon the death of .Captain O'Keefe. it
is stated, have been brought to the at
tention jof members of the Mazet Com
mittee, and the case will be fully in
quired into soon after the committee
resumes its sessions on May 31.
FOLLOWED THE DOG
TO A WATERY GRAVE
SAN RAFAEL. May 19.â A pathetic
little story is going the rounds in con
nection with the death .of Frank Grimm,
the popular young member of the Olympic
Club who recently met his death' in the
waters of the bay near Belvedere. It
seems that the"' Sunday before his un-
timely end the young man brought a little
blind terrier across the hay with the in
tention of putting the" animal out of its
misery. His intention became known, and
the fireman volunteered -to drown â¢ the
terrier, saying that would be the most
humane method to stop its suffering.
"Yes," . slowly, replied Grimm, "drown
ing is an easy death; let the poor old dog
go." \-
The terrier, was encompassed In a sack
and dropped Into he: bay at a point dis
tant only a few* hundred yards from the
spot where Grimm afterward sank. In a
few -'; days - â¢ the .-' sack - and *" contents Â« were
washed up on the shores of Angel Island.

SALE OF SAN JOSE
ELECTRIC SYSTEM
SAN JOSE, May 15.â The First-street
electric railway system was sold at pub
lic auction to-day to the German Savings
and Loan Society of San Francisco for
$225,000. The sale was a forced one under
foreclosure proceedings. There was a
large crowd present when Commissioner
T. E. Johnson began the sale, but only
one bidder appeared. That was George
Tourney, secretary of-*. he German Sav
ings and Loan Society, which held the
Issue of first-mortgage bonds of $200,000.
There was but one bid and it took but a
few minutes to close. the purchase.
There is also a second bond issue of
$150,000, secured by second mortgage, but
there remains not a dollar for these .bond
holders. In fact, the Oerman Savings and
Loan Society, the tirst mortgagees, will
lose some $.000 interest. Out of the $225.
--000 received from the road must come $5000
attorney fees for Jackson Hatch, $500 for
Commissioner Johnson, $9000 for three
years' salary for Receivers Alvord- and
Findlay at $125 per month each, $190 costs
of court and $2000 for repairs on receiver's
certificates. There was some $18,000 in
terest due the German Savings and Loan
Society. â¢
The First-street electric system is about
seventeen miles in length. It runs the en
tire distance of First street out to the
cemetery, with branches into the four
quarters* of the city. It was built by Ja
cob Rich about ten years ago at a cost
of $465,000. Rich became involved several
years ago and went into insolvency. At
that time receivers were appointed for
the road. It has just been about clearing
expenses for the past three years.
The road was immediately turned over
to Mr. Tourney and George L. Barker of
San Francisco was installed as manager.
No change, will be' made at present, but
many improvements are contemplated.
Mr. Tourney stated that while the Ger
man Savings and Loan Society bought
the road, they would not hold it. and he
and some associates would purchase it.
-There are a number of buyers after the
road. Henry S. Burke of the Santa Clara
and San Jose electric road wants It to
combine with their line. Hugh Center of
the Alum Rock motor line is another one
desirous of getting it. If he buys it his
line will connect with it and the power
changed to electricity, with branches to
Alviso and Campbell.
STRANGER ATTEMPTS SUICIDE.
MARYSVILLE, May 19.â A stranger
who gives his name as Jasper Young and
claims residence at lone attempted sui
cide to-day. As he was trudging- along
the railroad track between this city and
Live Oak he cut a gash in his throat with
a pocket knife, and as he walked into
the latter place the attention of the au
thorities was attracted to the wound,
which was bleeding freely. He was taken.
to Yuba City by a Constable and a com
mission in lunacy will investigate the
case, as' Young says he. has been .con
fined in one of the insane asylums. His
age is 40. A silver watch and $4 in coin
comprise his effects.

STREAM OF
GOLD FLOWS
TO WASTE
Midas Mining Company Loses
j Valuable Tailings and
Slu.c.s.
â â¢ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Special* Dispatch to The Call.
REDDING, May 19.â A report
reached this city to-day of a loss of
from $60,000 to $75,000 sustained by the
Midas Gold Mining Company at Har
rison Gulch by the breaking of a reser
voir containing many thousand cubic
feet of tailings and sluices. The ma
terial was in a liquid state of about the
consistency of syrup.
When the wall of the reservior gave
way the tailings and sluices poured into
Harrison Gulch Creek, thence into Bee
Gum and finally into the north fork of
Cottonwood, coloring the waters for a
distance of twenty miles from the
reservoir.
The Midas Gold Mining Company is
the largest mining concern operating
in Western Shasta. Its equipment con
sists Of a modern twenty-stamp mill
and a chlorination plant, and to this is
now being added a smelter. The tail
ings and sluices which were lost are
what go over the plates in the stamp
mill. Much precious metal is still re
tained and all of this material was be
ing stored awaiting the completion of
the smelter, when it would have been
roasted and then cyanided. The tail
ings reservoir covered more than half
an acre.
When the restraining wall gave way
nothing could be done to check the flow
of the uncoined money until the reser
voir had emptied itself of more than
half its contents. The sluices and tail
ings which reached the creek" will p_pve
a total 10-.s.
Captain J. H. . Roberts- of a river
transportation line with headquarters
in Sacramento, is the president and
principal stockholder of the Midas Gold
Mining Company. The company has
expended several hundred thousand
dollars at Harrison Gulch and is re
garded as having one. of the best prop
erties in the county. The mine yields
from $30,000 to $50 000 a month. The loss
will not affect the operations of the
mine or milling plant.

JUNEAU.. Alaska, May 12.â Captain W.
XV. Robinson, assistant quartermaster,
U. S. A., who has charge of the construc

Townsend, has arrived here on ,an impor
tant mission. It is understood he has or
ders to examine into the suitability and
practicability, for coast defense purposes
of the 1200 acres of land reserved by the
Government for a military post on the
peninsula between Chilkat Inlet and Lynn
Canal, where the town of Mission has
started to grow. Dp here it is considered
at admirable point of defense against the
powerful Chilkat Indians in case of fur
ther trouble, and also against the British
and Canadians in case of sanguinary dif
ferences over the coast boundar^- line of
Alaska.
Captain Robinson will also inspect the
': summit of White Pass, and make a- trip
to Sitka. The steamer Wolcott was char
tered for the trip. He was accompanied
by his brother. Commodore H. S. Robin
son. Alaska, superintendent of the Pacific
Coast Steamship Company. At Wrangel,
, on the way to Juneau. Captain Robinson
purchased, at a reported cost of $7000, the
light-draught sternwheel British boat
Duchesnay for use by the Captain Glenn
Government exploring party on the Shus
j hitna River, a large stream emptying
Into Cook Inlet. Captain Glenn's is one of
: the â  Government parties looking for an
| all-American route to the- Yukon basin.
Captain Robinson was stationed at the]
Presidio in -1596, and has many friends in !
; San Francisco.

IS BLUFFING

j Objects to the Dispatch of

â â â¦ â
Special IJispatch to The Call.
Special Dispatch . to The Call.
NEW YORK. May 19â The Herald's
NEW YORK, May 19.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent sends the
following: While pressing the United
States to agree to arbitration of the
Alaskan boundary controversy the
British Government has entered objec
tion to the dispatch of additional Amer
ican troops to that territory.
The controversy between the two
governments has assumed a more seri
ous aspect than is generally supposed.
While the President is bound to the
principle of arbitration and while, on
'â  its face, the case the United States
couid present would, it is believed, re-
sult in a triumph for this Government,
: there remains a possibility that the ar
bitration tribunal might decide that the
British Government is entitled to a port
i on the Pacific Coastâ perhaps* Skaguay,
, of which Great Britain desires to ob
! tain possession.
In; view of the great commercial In-
terests of the Pacific Coast of the
United States the administration does
: not feel justified in accepting arbitra
tion and the British Government has
, been given to understand that this is
the President's way of looking at the
â . proposition. So far as I have been able
j to learn, however, England continues
| to strongly press arbitration.
With respect to the dispatch of three
companies of regulars to Alaska to pa
: trol the boundary line, the purpose this
i Government had in view was to pre
-1 serve peace and prevent conflict be-
tween Canadian and American miners,
which reports from Governor Brady
show is likely to occur at any moment.
It is probable, however,, that in view
'. of Great Britain's objection the troops
I will not be - sent to Pyramid Harbor
as originally intended, but will be lo
cated at a point accessible to the dis
i puted territory, so as to be available in
case of emergency. Y.'::Y'Y
In its representation to the American
Government the British Government
! takes the view that in the dispatch of
troops to Alaska the United States pro-
poses to retain by force the territory
which it claims, and that .consequently
I it feels in the interest of friendly rela
i tions between the two governments
; they should not go.
Great Britain had indicated that she
; has no intention of sending troops to
; patrol her side of the boundary line,
; and the officials here have no reason to
; believe she will take such action. In
; fact Great Britain, like the United
: States, is extremely anxious to have
1 the controversy settled as promptly and
i as amicably as possible, but she appre
ciates the imperative need of an outlet
.on the Pacific Coast for her great
Northwest Territory, and she is bend
! ing every effort to this end.
It is apparent that on the settlement
of the Alaskan boundary dispute de
; pends the reassembling of the High
Joint Commission in August. The ad-
ministration would very much prefer
: if the boundary question were left to
this commission to be disposed of. It
iis pointed out that the two govern-
ments originally determined to submit
the question to the commission and
though the commission failed to reach
a decision a further discussion of the
matter might lead to satisfactory set-
tlement. There are ..several other mat-
ters which the commission is practi
cally agreed upon and a treaty pro-
viding for their adjudication could be
drawn up at this time, but the British
Government is averse to making any
concessions unless the Alaskan bound-
ary dispute is settled. In other words,
unless a port in Alaska is ceded to her
! she would prefer the continuance of the
status quo. . .

SANTA CRUZ. May 19.â One of the

SANTA BARBARA, May 19.-The case
of Mrs. Frances Broome, the woman who
made a vigorous defense of an evergreen
hedge about her property on Micheltorena
street, that the Marshal was ordered to
remove, and was arrested on a charge of
resisting an officer, came up this morning
before Judge Day In .the Superior Court.
The defendant again appeared without
counsel, and although In the presence of
a dozen leading lawyers she stated to the
court that none was satisfactory, for she
feared they were all prejudiced against
her. Mrs. Broome was accompanied by
Hon. Elwood toper this morning. The
Judge addressed Mrs. Broome, inquiring
If she was ready to proceed, and in reply
the defendant handed the court an un
sealed letter. It was an affidavit in Mrs.
Broome's handwriting, asking for a fur
ther continuance. It read as loliows:

Barbara vs.. Trances I'roome. the defendant

The usual attendance at a State Fair is
very large, and a great number of iwrsons
from abroad who are contemplating locating

hibit no objection will be made.
As to the character and extent of the county
exhibits, the board desires that this should
be left entirely to the discretion of the county
authorities making the exhibit. The exhibit
should co_ialn samples, or displays of the lead
ing and most valuable products of the re

small and Inexpensive or It may be made much

example, your people are largely interested in
fruit, green, dried and preserved, raisins,

of such size and extent, mounted or prepared
In such manner as your taste and Judgment dic
tate, would form a sufficient representation of
your agricultural wealth. >

county making them, but when all. prepared

map of California, and will be of such gen
eral benefit that it is to be hoped that every

ber of spectators.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAT 20, 1899.
O'TOOLE' COFFIN
CONTAINED ROCKS
Discovery of a Daring Fraud to
Cheat Several praterQal
Lodges.
FORT SCOTT, Kans., May _..âThe grave in which Dr. Thomas
O'Toole of this city was supposed to '. have been buried, in a remote spot
in Western Texas, on the. 22d of April last, was opened to-day, and, ac
cording to reports received here from the Texas authorities by the fra
ternal society in which he recently took out an $8000 life insurance, the
casket was found filled with rocks. wrapped in an old blanket.
O'Toole had a diploma from th.=, Chicago" Medical College and was a
manufacturer of, patent medicine and evidently making money. In the
past few months he took out $8000 Insurance in the Modern Woodmen,
Royal Neighbors and Woodmen of the World lodges in this city.- Two
months ago he went to Texas, ostensibly on business. April 21 his wife
was called to Sonora, . Texas, via San Angelo^ the nearest railroad sta
tion, by a telegram from one Dr.- Maguire, supposed to be the associate
of O'Toole, announcing the latter!s sickness. From Sonora she wired the
lodges. that her husband had died. and had been buried before her ar
rival there, and that on accountl of the quarantine she could not go to
the grave nor bring the body home. "Â£ ;'
The suspicion of the lodge officers was aroused and they instituted
an investigation, which . resulted to-day in the announcement that
O'Toole's casket was filled with rocks. The Texas authorities have ar
rested a man supposed to be the doctor, but information to-night from
Old Mexico announces his capture there. Upon receipt of the news of
the disclosures here Mrs. O'Toole'fled with her two children. The family
had been in deep mourning since the doctor's death was reported and
seemed greatly afflicted. The doctor and his family had lived here but
a few years and are thought to have come from Mexico.
SIX TO SIX THE
JURY REMAINED
Failed to Agree in the
Murdock Case.
â â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WILLOWS, May 19.â After twenty
four hours' confinement in the jury
room at the Courthouse the twelve men
sworn to try the great Murdock note
case Were discharged at _ o'clock this
afternoon, having failed to agree. After
receiving the instructions of the court
yesterday the jury immediately re
paired to the juryroom and upon the
first ballot stood six to six, and re
mained that way until discharged.
It was evident the jurymen had a
warm argument after retiring. They
kept the Sheriff busy bringing in ice
water, and when the door was opened
two of the jurymen could be seen in
their shirt sleeves and there was evi
dence that a heated argument was in
progress. .-, â¢
j The principals in the case, together
I with their attorneys,, remained in the
courtroom adjacent the jury room until
a late hour last night and all day,
; eagerly expectant that a verdict would
be rendered. The jurymen were taken
j to breakfast this morning and to din
: ncr at 1 o'clock by the Sheriff. . -
An interview by Tho Call correspond-
ent of the jurors gives but little infor
i mation, as those in favor claim those
opposed ignored the evidence entirely,
and no amount of questioning will
bring any reasons for their position
other than improbability.
Expert testimony, however, seems to
have cut hilt little figure in the matter,
yet a fortune has been expended by the
: defense on that line. Attorney Free-
I man, for the plaintiff, as soon as the
: court discharged the jury, moved the
court to set the case for trial immedi
: ately. ' The court said he would enter-
tain such a motion on a week from
Monday. The work of seven long weeks
seems to have gone for naught, yet the
plaintiff's , attorneys seem . in good
spirits, claiming a victory, and are
| ready for another round. ; â¢ .Â;

GERMAN WARSHIP
GOES ON A REEF
The Kormoran, Ordered to Samoa in
a Hurry, Runs to Australia
for Repairs.
for Repairs. \i v.
/VANCOUVER, B. ('.. May 19.-The Ger
man warship Kormoran will not take
part in the Samoan difficulties for some
time to come. . She was ordered in a
hurry for Samoa by the German Admir
alty from China. En route she called at
German New Guinea, and after, leaving
there ran clean on Whirlwind reef, plain
ly marked on the chart and known as a
dangerous spot to all navigators. For
twenty-four hours the commander was at
his wits' ends, but finally had both masts
cut away and ail her machinery, the
steering gear and refrigerator .machine
jettisoned. The ship went on March 24
and was hauled off on the 31st, being high
and dry for nix days and nights. Just as
it had been decided to consign a few of
her guns to the deep she was floated. She
is now in Morts' dock, Sydney. In the
dock she shows the. keel completely torn
away and her bottom badly damaged
Commander Emsrhan is the sole survivor
of the Elbe in the hurricane off Samoa
1889. He fought the Samoans ten years
ago and many of his crew were butch
ered. He says they are the most treach
erous people on the face of the- earth.

TAXED WITH
TRYING TO STEAL
A BIG FRANCHISE
Sanitary Reduction
Works Accused.
â â¢ â
CHARGED' WITH BAD FAITH
-...,: . .. __ â^_ â . . . .^
â â¦ â
GARRET McENERNEY ATTACKS
A' PROPOSED ORDINANCE.
â¦
Declares That Under the Guise of an
Declares That Under the Guise of an
Amendment the Corporation Is
Trying" to Gain a
Monopoly.
Are the owners of the Sanitary Reduc
tion Works trying to steal a valuable
franchise from the city? Attorney R. B.
Mitchell, representing the corporation,
says that it is not. Attorney Garret W.
McEnerney, representing the Scavengers'
Union, declares that it is. Not only does
he so declare, but he gives day and date
for all that he has to say on the subject
and refuses to qualify his declaration by
so much as a word. '
The arguments of the learned attor
neys were presented to the Health and
Police Committee of the Board of Super
visors yesterday afternoon, the occasion
being the consideration by the commit
tee of the blanket ordinance introduced at
the meeting of the board last Monday at
the instance of the owners of the Sani
tary Reduction Works. The proposed
ordinance purports to be an amendment
to the existing ordinance â¢ compelling
householders and others to cause all
garbage and refuse of whatever descrip
tion to be conveyed to the crematory of
the Reduction Works and to pay for its
destruction by the sanitary methods in
vogue there. It differs from the existing
ordinance only in that it provides that
the Reduction Works shall not charge
to exceed 20 cents per cubic yard for
destroying the garbage in its furnaces.
It is this apparently innocent and inof
fensive amendment that has caused all of
the trouble and led to the serious charges
made by Mr. McEnerney.
Mr. Mitchell, on behalf of the corpora
tion, explained to the members of the
committee that what was asked of the
board in the way of an amendment to the
existing ordinance was really not of great
consequence to the corporation, but was
of prime importance to the community
at large, as it provides for their protec
tion by naming the maximum price they
can be forced to pay for the destruction
of their garbage. He. rehearsed at some
length the history of the corporation
from its inception to the present day, and
declared it to be a distinctly modern and
civilizing improvement, which should re
ceive the approval and support of all good
citizens not to mention the members of
the Board of Supervisors.
When Mr. McEnerney was accorded the
privilege of the floor he lost no time in
coming to the point. "We claim," he
said, by way of introduction, "that an
attempt is being made to steal a franchise
that nets the Sanitary Reduction Works
the sum of $50,000 a year on an investment
of $150,000. The Sanitary Reduction Works
is here asking this, board to grant it free
of charge a privilege that will permit it
to- levy tribute on the people' of this city
for fifty years to come."
This was sufficiently startling, but Mr.
McEnerney was not . done. He repeated
that the corporation was attempting to
steal a franchise and then went ahead
and proved the truth of his assertion. He
explained that the question of the valid
ity of .the franchise under which the cor
poration is operating is now pending in
the United States, Circuit Court before
Judge Morrow; that one of the points
upon which the validity of the franchise
is attacked is that it makes no provision
for the maximum price that may be
charged by the corporation for the incin
eration of garbage, and that therefore
the people are at the mercy of the cor
poration and have not the protection that
is guaranteed them by law.
"While this question is pending." he
said, "and before it is determined whether
or not the corporation has a franchise,
they sneak in here and ask this board to
pass an ordinance that will repair the
very defect upon which our contention is
founded. Should this board pass the pro
posed ordinance and if on the. following
day Judge Morrow should declare the cor
poration's franchise Invalid, what would
be the result? The corporation would
laugh in the faces of the citizens, whom
they seek to bleed. This proposed ordin
ance would then be their franchise and
the hands of the people would be tied.
Their present existence is due to collu
sion between the attorneys of the cor
poration and the members of the last
Board of Supervisors. Now they seek to
hoodwink the members of this board and
secure themselves in advance against an
adverse decision â  which they have cause
to believe will be rendered by . the
courts. â¢ We ask that no action bo
taken by the board in this matter
until the validity of the corpora
tion's franchise has been finally passed
upon by the courts, or* at least that the
board will submit the question of the
validity of the existing ordinance to tho
City and County Attorney before any
action is taken." *-.-'-.'
1 In reply. Mr. Mitchell denied that the â 
revenue of the corporation which he rep
resents is anything like $50,000. He said
that all public improvements were subject
to adverse' criticism, and he asked that
the committee be not led away by the
eloquence of the learned gentleman who
represented the other side.
After some deliberation the committee
decided to postpone final action for one
week. ;

| holding a meet to-day. One Herbert

knowing ones to get a sanction from the

fense and gave him the necessary sane-
tion.

up their efforts to win Mr. Stephens ovet

ed to get hold of just what was going on
among the fruit growers. There was not

As gracefully as 'it might, the
Southern Pacific has admitted the
charge made by The Call that it was
he instigator of the persistent at
tempts made by its agents on the
Railroad Commission, Biackstock and
Laumeister, to dismiss the grain rate
cases.
Yesterday Mr. Herrin sent Attor
ney Pillsbury before the Federal
court to apply for a dismissal and
relieve his hirelings of the odium of
a reiteration of their former demands
and the insult they paid the court
upon the first occasion.
Upon the request of the plaintiff
Judge Morrow dismissed the cases,
and the Railroad Commission is now
free to promulgate and enforce, if it
may. the new freight schedule which
Commissioner Laumeister has been
preparing under the direction of Mr.
Stubbs.
Th.' railroad grain case was dismissed
by Judge Morrow yesterday in the United
States Circuit Corurt on motion of E. S.
Pillsbury of counsel for the Southern Pa
cific Company.
United States Attorney Coombs had
not been notified that the motion was
about to be made, and would not have
been present in court had not Assistant
United States Attorney "Woodworth seen
the railroad attorney quietly making his
way into the courtroom. He immediately
notified Mr. Coombs, and he was in at- !
tendance when the motion to dismiss'
came up. The complainant was not re
quired, however, to give notice to the
legal representative of the United States
in the suit. -

Mr. Pillsbury, in stating his motion, re
ferred to the suggestion made the other
day by Judge Morrow when the State's
Attorney General asked for a dismissal
by request of the respondents, that such
a motion should properly come from the
complainant, and added that he agreed
with the court in that suggestion. He
therefore presented the following:

Judge Morrow thereupon made the fol
lowing order: "The complainant having on
this, the 19th day of May, _99, produced
and filed in open court its dismissal of
this suit, it is hereby ordered and decreed
that the said cause be and the same is
hereby dismissed."
Mr. Pillsbury thereupon took his hat
and withdrew.
It Is the practice in cases wherein the
motion for dismissal is made by the com
j plainant for the respondent to file within
five days its bill of costs. These costs in-
elude court fees, clerk's; stenographer's
and marshal's fees, but do not include
attorneys' fees, which will be the biggest
Item in the bill.
Attorney General Ford stated to a re-
porter that he was not in court during
the* proceedings. The news that the case
had been- dismissed came as a surprise
to him. as he had not been notified nor
consulted with in reference *tb the mat
ter. He would collect every dollar of
costs coming to the State, and would im
mediately consult with Messrs. Foote and
Hayne as to the manner in which the
State's interests should be guarded in
the matter of its costs.
E. S. Pillsbury, attorney for the rail
road, hazarded an explanation of his
action which did not clearly explain.
Asked why he yesterday moved for a dis
missal of the cases when but ten days I
before he had pointedly ignored Judge
Morrow's suggestion to do so, he said:
"When Attorney General Ford first
moved to dismiss these cases I was taken
by surprise and declined to do so solely
for the reason that I wished time to dis-
cuss the proposition with mv colleagues
and clients.
"Since that time the Railroad Commis
sioners, the defendants in the case, have
adopted a resolution rescinding the La
Rue resolution, the original basis for the
suits, and another instructing the Attor
ney General to ask for a dismissal of the
suits as they now stand. For these rea
sons the company decided that further
litigation would be unnecessary, the rea-
son for it being removed by action of the
commission. As a v consequence, it in-
structed me to ask for a dismissal to-
day.
"As to the costs, we claim that we have
a right to reimbursement for what we
have already expended, but to that we
waive our right and are willing to stand
on the order of the court for an assess
ment.
We consider that this dismissal not
only dissolves the Injunction, but also
throws the cases out of court."
received as a patient some time ago. suf
fering from a cold. He partially recov
ered and was able to be out the first part
of the week, but within the past couple
of days he returned to his ward and be
came seriously ill. The surgeons were
startled to discover traces of smallpox
and immediately notified the authorities
As soon as possible the hospital was
placed in quarantine and every precau
tion to prevent the spread of the disease
taken.
Shortly after C o'clock last evening Mc-
Murray was taken to Fort Point near the
artillery barracks. Accompanying him
was a detachment of twelve men. There
he will remain in charge of Captain Isaac
P. Ware, assistant surgeon, U. S. A., and
a nurse, both of whom will be isolated
with him. The hospital will remain in
quarantine until it has been thoroughly
fumigated.
The patient is reported to be doing nice
ly and his recovery is certain. No appre
hension of additional cases is felt at the
reservation. The case is believed to be
sporadic.
HANGED HIMSELF ON
CLARENDON HEIGHTS
JOHN KLOPFEB, COOPER, SEEKS
DEATH.
DEATH.
Just back of the Spring Valley reser
voir, off Corbett road, the lifeless body
of a man was found swinging from the
limb of a tree at about 4 o'clock yester
day afternoon by a man who refuses to
give his name. The Coroner's office was
notified and Deputy Gately responded to
the call. He cut down the body and re
moved it to the Morgue. The only prop
erty found on the body was the following
letter, written in German:
My Dear Good-Hearted Woman and Honest
Wife: I cannot stand this sad condition any
longer. It is very hard on me to do this bad
deed, especially to leave you In hard circum-
stances. Dear Emll and Ferdinand, be good to
your dear mother, because she Is a good mother
to you, and how foolish I have treated her. It
is entirely my fault that I leave you In such
circumstances, and for this reason I can't live
any longer. If I could get workâ but I can get
nothing to do so I am tired of life. .Many best
wishes and kisses to your dear mother and my
dear children. The good-for-nothing cigar
manufacturer, Frledlander, .on Montgomery
street, is the cause of It. May the wrath of
heaven come upon him. JOHN KLOPFER.
Late last night a young man visited the
Morgue and recognized the body as that
of his father, John Klopfer, of the firm
of Waechtel & Klopfer, coopers, at 2214
Folsom street. He said that his father
was 50 years of age, born in Germany and
resided at .25 Broderick street. Further
than this he declined to give any informa
tion, except that his father was at home
Thursday night. He said he never heard
of the man Friediander mentioned In the
letter.
The "man Friediander" proved to be
Louis Friediander of 431 Montgomery
street. He says that he thinks Klop.er
was slightly demented. About ten years
ago Friediander, Klopfer and others
bought up a block of land at auction
Shortly afterward the abstract of title
was lost and a few months ago Klopfer
came to Friediander and told him that he
could not find the abstract and had been
informed that his property was heavily
mortgaged. A few days later he asked
Frledlander to purchase the property at a
price which Investigation -showed was far
below its true market value. Friediander
did not care- to enter Into the transaction
and advised Klopfer to have the title
searched and satisfy his mind that all was
right. The two paid several visits to a
real estate office and then Klonfer disap
peared and Frledlander did not hear from
him until he received the news of his
death.
Dispute Over Property.
Stilt was filed yesterday by. the Sacra
mento Bank against Charles Montgom
ery, Richard H. McDonald Jr. and O. F.
Miner to quiet title to a portion of West
ern Addition block 438. In the complaint
It it* alleged that Montgomery claims
to hold the property in trust for McDon
ald. The bank asks to be declared the
sole owner thereof and that the defend
ants have no right or title therein.

MORE VESSELS
BUILDING FOR
SUGAR TRADE
Launching of the
Robert R. Hind.
Another new four-masted schooner has
been added to the Hind, Rolph & Co.
sugar fleet. The new boat is the Robert
R. Hind, and she was launched from the
shipyards at Alameda a few days ago.
She was christened by Miss Mildred
Rolph, and Is a sister ship to the Honolpu
and Muriel. The latter are two of the
smartest vessels of their class afloat, and
if the new boat can beat them she will be
a crackajack. The Hind is 102 feet 8 inches
long, 37 feet 8 inches beam and 13 feet
deep, and will carry 700,000 feet of lumber
or 1100 tons of sugar. ;.;?;-
The first vessel built by Hind, Rolph &
Co. was the Muriel. She was soon fol
lowed by the Honolpu and the Robert R.
Hind, and in a few weeks the schooner \
James Bolph and the four-masted bark- 'â 
entine "William Carson, now nearlng com
pletion at Eureka, will be added to the
fleet. Besides these vessels the firm owns
the schooners Defender, John G. North,
G. W. Watson and barkentine Wrestler.
The Robert R. Hind and the James
Rolph are exactly alike, and the first time
they meet there is going to be an inter-
-sting race. That is not likely to take
place until the latter part of the year, |
however, as the James Rolph will go from
Eureka to Australia, while, the Robert R. j
Hind will load here for Hawaii.
Captain J. S. Hellingson. late of the j
schooner John A, will command the Rob- j
crt R. Hind; Captain Piltz, late of the |
barkentine Charles F. Crocker, will com
mand the William Carson, and Captain
Dedrick, late of the Defender, will com- I
mand the James Rolph.
There were several mishaps during the
loading of the Sacramento River steamer j
Aurora yesterday, and in consequence a i
wagon load of furniture and another of j
tombstones are at the bottom of the bay.
It' was low water at the time, and the |

George S. Long, a porter in the employ
of Well & Co., on Sanscme street, was
seriously injured in an elevator accident.
While trying to get a barrel of whisky
on the elevator it started and jammed
him against one of the supports. His
left arm was broken and he received two
severe contused wounds over the eve. He
was treated at the Harbor Hospital, and
will be around again in a few days.

Stopped the Brandy.
Deputy Surveyor St. John seized yester
day forty cases of blackberry brandy on
board the steamer Catherine Sudden con
signed to C. Lombard at St. Michael,
Alaska, by the California Fruit Juice
Company. The consignors had received
permission from the surveyor to ship ten
cases of blackberry brandy to St.. Michael
for medicinal use. but when the stuff
was put on board Mr. St. John found that
the supposed ten cases were ten crates,
each containing four cases of brandy.
Mr. St. Jphn-made some remarks about
commercial honesty and put a stop to the
shipment.
