SERIOUS ROW
AT THE HOTEL
SUTHERLAND
Mrs. Josephine Burr
on Trial.
There was an air of refinement In Judge
Mogan's courtroom yesterday morning
that was a surprise to the regular ha
bitues. It looked as if a number of so
ciety ladies had instituted a new order of
things to be designated a Police Court
function. It turned out, however, that
the gathering of ladies was due to the
case, of Mrs. Josephine Burr, who was
charged by Miss M. E. Sturtevant with
the unpardonable sin of using vulgar lan
guage in the Hotel Sutherland on April 2*.
According to Miss Sturtevant, the com
plaining witness, Mrs. Burr had accused
her of showing the Burr room to a gen
tleman who had called to engage rooms.
This roused Mrs. Burr, and ..irs. Gean
drot, the landlady, who was sick in bed,
sent for Mrs. Burr to talk the matter
over.
Mrs. Burr testified that she had ex
pressed her displeasure at the board, esÂ»
pecially the butter, and also the way in
which the bathroom was kept, and when
she heard that Miss Sturtevant, whom
she designated as the "bouncer" at the
hotel, had been showing her (Mrs. Burr's)
room to a gentleman, she felt annoyed
and spoke to her about it. She was asked
to see Mrs. Geandrot, who was sick in
bed, and acc6rdlngly she went to her bed
room.- Miss Sturtevant was in an ad
joining room with the door open, and Mrs.
de Verna was in the room at the other
side with the door also open, so as to
hear what was going on, and some ladies
stood outside in the hallway. After talk
ing the matter over. Mrs. Geandrot lost
her temper and said Mrs. Burr had been
driven out of the Hotei Lenox, and then
Mrs. Geandrot called Mrs. Burr an offen
sive name. Mrs. Burr retorted that Mrs.
oeandrot was in the position of not being
able to throw stones at her neighbors
in that respect, and then Miss Sturte
vant came in and forcibly ejected Mrs.
Burr. She left the house two days later.
The Judge, after hearing the evidence
of Miss Beatrice Burr, said: "It would
take a Solomon to decide this case. It
appears to be a row among women and
I can't. tell which side is right. As Mrs.
Burr has left the hotel after paying her
bill, I think the best thing I can do is to
dismiss the case."
COMPANY D
CARRIES OFF
FIRST HONORS
League of the Cross
Drill.
â â¢ â
CADETS IN COMPETITION
CADETS IN COMPETITION
â * â
GRAND PARADE BY THE ENTIRE
GRAND PARADE BY THE ENTIRE
FIRST REGIMENT.
- â â .
A Large C**owd at Mechanics' Pavil-
A Large C^owd at Mechanics' Pavil-
ion Loudly Applauds the In-
tricate Movements of the
Ycung Soldiers.
Company D, League of the Cross Ca
dets, of Sacred Heart Parish, carried off
the honors last night at Mechanics' Pa
vilion in the fifth annual competitive
drill of the First Regiment of that organ
ization. Two other companies competed
for the officers' trophy, A of St. Mary's
Parish and H of St. Brendan's.
The seating capacity of tne Pavilion,
both on the main floor and in the gal
leries, was tested to the full capacity, and
no prettier scene could be imagined than
that presented' when the full regiment,
headed by Casassa's band and the League
of the Cross fife, drum and bugle corps,
made its appearance on the floor and
passed in review before the stand. In
the absence of Major General Shatter,
who was too ill to attend, the regular
army was represented in the reviewing
stand by Captain R. H. Noble of General
Shatter's staff, and Lieutenants R. A.
Brown and T. H. Slavens of the Fourth
Cavalry, who had consented to act as
judges of the drill. Major General Dick
inson and Brigadier General Warfield, of
the .National Guard, and their staffs oc
cupied places of honor in the stand. The
only civilian with them was Mayor Phe
lan. ';' â â '."'â '' Y" ;' '.â¢'â 'â '"
After the review and inspection con
ducted by Major General Dickinson the
regiment marched off and a stirring num
ber by the band filled in the interval be
fore the appearance of Company A on
the floor ready to go through the evolu
tions of the drill prepared for the occa
sion by Colonel Sullivan. When the com
pany did come on, headed by Captain Wil
liam C. Hopper, the cheers showed its
popularity and the presence of a large
crowd of well-wishers, whose hopes of
victory seemed well based as the young
men moved off in the first figures of the
drill.
â¢Before the appearance of the next com
peting company there- was a spectacular
skirmish drill by Company D, Captain
Dwyer, the men in campaign uniform,
followed by an exhibition of the hospital
corps showing the manner of rendering
first aid to the wounded on the field.
Company H of St. Brendan's parish fol
lowed in the effort to capture the prize in
the competition. It was in command of
Captain J. Power and was composed of a
splendid looking lot of young fellows, who
seemed to the manner born. They went
at the work cut out for them as though it
were an everyday matter, and barring a
few bobbles in the beginning seemed to
have struck a. winning gait, which they
maintained to the end, retiring from the
floor to the music of the great bell in the
center of the Pavilion, which had been
set dinging by one of their admirers.
Company G of St. Brlgid's parish, rest
ing on the laurels gained in three preced
ing competitive drills, contented itself
with an exhibition saber drill that was
almost perfection. It cut and slashed to
a man in the most approved style, and
parried and carted and tierced and dug
holes in the pavilion floor with its sabers
until the vast crowd applauded again and
again. Then it was lined up in company
front and the great flag was unfurled
from the roof to its salute.
By this time Company D had donned
its parade dress and was marched past
the stand and lined up for the inspec
tion of the judges before it began what
proved to be the winning movement of
the competition. It was commanded by
Captain I. D. Dwyer, with George H.
Sullivan first,- lieutenant and Vincent Car
roll second lieutenant.. The members of
this company were more youthful look
ing than their competitors, and although
they seemed to possess the grace and con
fidence necessary to carry off the prize,
there were not many who thought they
would. They had not been long at drill
when this doubt was succeeded by a cer
tainty that they "were the people."
It was quite noticeable that movements
that had seemed tame and commonplace
when executed by their predecessors .on
the floor were fairly electrifying when
performed by the boys of D. They put
a vim and a dress and a finish into the
drill that was so soul-satisfying that it
was not necessary for the. last number
to convince nearly everybody present that
the decision would go to the young fel
lows from Sacred Heart parish. They
were so sure of it themselves that when
they marched off they surrounded their
captain and saluted him with dTawn sa
bers and three times three cheers.
Awaiting the decision of the Judges the
entire regiment was assembled, and un
der the command. of Lieutenant Colonel
Thomas F. Ryan marched in parade, at
the conclusion Colonel Sullivan announc
ing that the judges had found in favor of
Company D. The winning command then
marched on and saluted, and Mayor Phe
lan, acting in the stead of Archbishop
Riordan. who was too ill to appear, pinned
the trophy on to the breast of Captain
Dwyer. and the competition was over. The
band played "We Won't Go Home Till
Morning," which some one - remarked
was rather odd for a temperance organi
zation, and everybody made for the cars.
One number was cut from the pro
gramme. This was the mustering in of
the entire regiment as an organization into
the League of the Cross Cadets. So much
time had been taken up by the drill that
It was considered impossible to perform
the ceremony in good season and it was
postponed to another occasion.
EPWORTH LEAGUE CONVENTION
May Come to San Francisco for Its
Next Session.
Extraordinary efforts will be made to
bring the 1901 International Epworth
League Convention to this city, and there
Is every indication that the undertaking
will be a success. Two . committees are
now actively at work in this city
and the other in Los Angelesâ and they
will both have one object in view, and
that is to have the immense gathering
come to California. The local commit
tee met last night in the Young Men's
Christian Association building to formu
late plans and make preliminary arrange-
ments.
The San Francisco committee, consist
ing of Rev. Thomas Filben. D.D., C. B.
Perkins, Robert Husband, XV. H. Waste
and Miss S. E. Powell, at its meeting laat
night decided to begin active work to se
cure the convention for this State, and a
sub-committee was appointed to confer
with the municipal and commercial bodies
and invoke their aid in the movement. It
was stated that $25,000, necessary for the
proper entertainment of the visiting dele
gates, will be forthcoming and has all
been. practically subscribed.
The- committee, which has established
headquarters in the Y. M. C. A. building
in this city, has full power to act, and it
will be backed by the State, district and
city organizations. The campaign will in
volve the consultation and capture of the
leaders of the league In the United States
and Canada. The railroad companies,
recognizing the benefits of the Christian
Endeavor convention held here in 1897,
are co-operating with the committee, and
have guaranteed a â  low rate. It is ex
pected that there will be as many people
brought here in 1901 as in'lS97.

HE THREATENED
TO USE HIS GUN
ON THE OFFICER
Bakulich's Exciting
Experience.
â  â â¦ â
TAKEN FOR A "BAD MAN"
TAKEN FOR A "BAD MAN"
â â¦ â
HELD UP BY A STRANGER AT
LOS GATOS.
â¢
Both Men Arrested and Charged Wit-
Both Men Arrested and Charged With
Battery â The Police-
man Jumped His
Bail.
1
Police Officer/Bakulich, the Sandow of
the department, had an exciting experi
ence in Los Gatos several days, ago. Be
ing taken for a "bad man" he was held
up at the point of a pistol and was threat
ened with death if iie made any resist
ance. iTA;.?
Convinced that the man at the "other
end of the gun" meant business Baku
lich knocked him down and after a short
struggle succeeded in disarming him.
Bakulich promptly handcuffed his assail
ant and took him to the railroad depot,
where he turned him over to the con
stable.
As fiakulich's prisoner was bleeding
from the nose the Constable also took the
police officer into custody. At the . City
Jail they were both charged with battery.
Bakulich fortunately had $10 with him
and he was released. Several farmers,
thinking Bakulich had assaulted the other
defendant without provocation, gathered
about the jail determined to wreak venge
ance on the "bully," as they termed
him. The Constable, after learning that
Bakulich was a San Francisco police of
ficer, apologized for his mistake, and ad
dressing the revengeful farmers who
were gathering about the jail, informed
them that the "fellow was all right."
"He's a copper from Frisco," explained
the Constable. "The other fellow tried to
put it on him, but he got the worst of it."
Bakulich was then allowed to depart,
after notifying the Constable that he
would rather forfeit his $10 than stay in
town another day.
Bakulich, when seen last night, said: "I
was on my vacation, and not having any
place- to go. agreed to accompany my
friend, E. Chiclovich, of 2217 Fillmore
street, to Los Gatos. Chielovich is the
owner of some Los Gatos property, which
is being unlawfully held by a man named
T. N. Whitehead. He is the man with
whom I had the trouble. Some time, ago,
so Chielovich claims, Whitehead attacked
him and nearly choked him to death.
Chielovich determined to compel White
head to move his belongings from the
property and asked me to assist him. We
went to Los Gatos and posted a number
of notices announcing that the property
was for sale. After we had left the place
and were on our way to the railroad sta
tion Whitehead appeared and proceeded
to assault Chielovich. I told him to desist
and he made a blow at me. I seized him
and in the struggle he tore off my neck
tie. Just then he drew a revolver and,
placing it against my breast, threatened
to make me a subject for the Coroner. I
thought he meant what he said, so
I struck him in the face and knocked him
down. I then disarmed him and after
handcuffing him took him to the railroad
station, where we were both placed under
arrest.
"Whitehead evidently took me for a
bully, for when he accosted me he re
marked that even if Chielovich had a
bodyguard he could lick both of us. I
did not anticipate any trouble in Los
Gatos or I would not have consented to
accompany my friend Chielovich.
"Rather than lay over for another day
to be tried I notified the Constable that
he could forfeit my ball."
Town Talk.
. Wielders of the chips and cardboards
will be interested in the article by Pro
fessor Alfred Bengon in to-day's Town
Talk, the gilded gambling hells of Europe
being described from personal experi
ence. Some clever stories are woven into
the article about notabilities abroad. The
musical and dramatic reviews in this
number are of uncommon Interest; no
other weekly gives such space to musical
matters. The Saunterer discusses so
ciety, the clubs and politics, giving exclu
sive news. The half-tones in this Issue
are very fine, including persons popular
in politics, the drama and in the mercan
tile and music worlds. The editorials are
timely, and one upon public school teach
ers will attract attention from members
of the department. *.
National Guard Veterans.
The annual meeting of Veterans N. G.
C. was held at headquarters, 24 Fourth
street, last evening. An election of offi
cers was held, resulting in the choice of
ex-Private George R. Sanderson for com
mander; Major George Whitney Reid, re
tired, vice commander; ex-Lieutenant
James Pennycook, adjutant; Captain
John E. Klein, retired,- paymaster. The
corps is in a flourishing condition, both
financially and numerically. The annual
banquet is to be held at the Occidental
Hotel Wedneseday evening, June 1.

THE PANHANDLE EXTENSION.
THE project for the extension of the beautiful
entrance to the Golden Gate Park to Market
street has fairly attracted public attention, and
the more it is considered the better are the prospects
for its success. When popular interest in such an
improvement has once been excited the battle is half
won. because each day's consideration of its merits
develops new reasons for its indorsement, whether
it be regarded from an esthetic or from a utilitarian
point of view.
The citizens of San Francisco are justly proud of
their great park, which ha's not only proved a local
attraction and exerted a most beneficial influence
upon the community, but has won the admiration of
American and foreign visitors till its praises have
been sounded throughout the world. But, though
excellent and disinterested management and artistic
skill have already wrought wonders by the conver
sion of a barren solitude into a scene of bewildering
and variegated beauty, it must be remembered that
the work inconstantly in progress and is not half
done. It will be many years before the marvelous
picture now spreading over the shifting sands that
fringe the Pacific Ocean is completed. When it- lias
stretched almost to the edge of the spent breakers
that through every gradation from soothing murmurs
to angry wars illustrate the attracting moods of the.
great deep, it will be simply incomparable. So far
as we are advised there is no large city on this con
tinent or in Europe that can thus concentrate the
most exquisite phases of nature upon a combined
national and municipal boundary, which, it may be
observed, marking the Western limit of this repub
lic, connects the highest development of fraternity on
the land with that vaster fraternity of the sea, where
governments as well as men are equal in right and
equal in opportunity.
The creation of Golden Gate Park has already
stimulated the liberality of opulent citizens to an ex
tent that will be felt morally and intellectually for cen
turies. It is unnecessary to recapitulate the lavish
gifts of money that have aided the labors of the Park
Commissioners. But, when the invitation to this
educational and sanitary opportunity is extended to
the heart of our metropolis, and the toiling thousands
of our industrial population inhale the soliciting
breath of restful and purifying nature almost at their
doors, the response will be so general and so con
stant that local munificence will search the globe for
new attractions to perfect its relations with appre
ciative humanity.
There are utilitarian views of the panhandle ex
tension that should not be overlooked. The con
demnation of land and buildings tor the new boule
vard will put a large amount of money in circulation
and furnish employment for many laborers and ar
tisans. The improvement itself will also enhance the
value of residence property over a large and impor
tant section of the municipality. This branch of the
subject will require further elaboration. But at pres
ent it is sufficient to observe that the pressure of
sound public opinion is rapidly silencing ultra
conservative opposition. When capital and labor are
united in the advocacy of a wise public expenditure
the combination is irresistible.
An unexpected gleam of intelligence has at last
shot through the heads of the Piute Indians who. are
holding high carnival on the Pyramid Lake reser
vation. The Piutes can stand almost anything in the
way of imposition, but they have rebelled unani
mously against bookmakers on horse races.
The highbinders of Chinatown are sharpening their
hatchets for new victims. Fifty Chinese gambling
dens are in almost open operation. The Celestial lot
tery dealers are plying their trade with renewed
energy. Recent police reforms in the district. seem to
have acted as an inspiration.
The authorities at Washington are inclined to treat
â¢lightly the stoning of the American Consul at
.Manaos as Brazil is very friendly with the United
States. Perhaps the injured Consul entertains an
opinion that there are some case* where one ought to
he protected from his friends.
The Populists are taking steps to prevent losing
themselves in the next national campaign. With a
hope that they may be found somewhere after elec
tion day they have repudiated, all allegiance to the
cause of William Jennings Bryan. - * -;
SAN FRANCISCO AND DEWEY.
SAN FRANCISCO will have a monument to
Dewey, and it will be of a character sufficiently
noble and artistic to rank among the best ex-
amples of American monumental architecture of our
time. That much appears well assured by the re-
sponse which the public has given to the suggestion
of The Call that the erection of the monument should
be undertaken.
As was to have been expected, the proposal bf the
enterprise has been followed at once by considerations
as to where it shall be placed. That, however, is a
matter that can be postponed for a time. The choice
of site will form a part of the artistic problem of de-
signing the monument, for the one of course must
be appropriate to the other. All in due time these
matters will be considered, and the decision, it can
hardly be doubted, will be satisfactory to the con- j
tributors to the fund and to the general public.
It is to be noted that every one who has given the j
subject the consideration it merits recognizes the fit- j
ness of making San Francisco the place where Dewey's
victory is to receive its most enduring memorial and
the hero himself his most notable monument. More-
over, all concede that it will be far better for Cali-
formians to contribute to the erection of a monument
here than to the purchase of a mansion at Washing-
ton. The monument will be essentially a manifesta
tion of Western loyalty and will be a heritage of]
the people for centuries to come, while the Washing- :
ton mansion will but scantily represent the West, and,
moreover, will have nothing of the nature of an en-
during memorial.
Now that .the movement has been started and is j
under the direction of an able committee appointed
jby the Mayor, the next thing is to carry it forward
with a speed sufficient to generate enthusiasm as it
goes. The time is propitious to the undertaking. It
is but a year since the great victory was won, and j
every incident of the glorious triumph is fresh in the
minds of all. We have but recently celebrated with
an exuberant joy and loyalty the festival of Dewey j
day. and the ardor of the occasion has hardly yet
passed away. The public mind, therefore, may be
said to be already attuned to the enterprise, and its \
responsiveness when rightly appealed to will be
found cordial, prompt and generous.
The standard of excellence to be attained by the
monument should be set high. Nothing commonplace
or of an insignificant size will do. Dewey's victory
opens before San Francisco a broad pathway to com-
mercial empire over the greatest of the oceans of the
earth. That path has been opened at a time when the
Pacific is beginning to be the field of the greatest
activity of the expanding energies of the nations. It
made this republic known as a world power and one
that from this time forth is to be reckoned with in all |
questions affecting Pacific Ocean affairs. So great
an achievement cannot be fitly marked by a monu-
"*
mental structure of anything less than the highest
order.
WHAT IS TREASON?
THE question again becomes pertinent. The
constitution defines treason, but in such a way
as to leave the freedom of discussion untouched.
American citizens have always been free to discuss
public policies, because it is only by such discussion
that the foundations of the Government can be main
tained. When the Spanish war was undertaken to
remove the obstacles presented by Spanish sov
ereignty to the desire of millions of people to be free,
no one dreamed of any change in the institutions or
purposes of this republic.
The struggle for independence in Cuba began in
1895. The national platforms of 1896 dealt with it
from a strictly and praiseworthy American stand
point. The Republican platform said: "From the
hour of achieving their own independence the peo
ple of the United States have regarded with sympathy
the struggles of the other American peoples to free
themselves from European domination. We watch
with deep and abiding interest the heroic battle of
the Cuban patriots against cruelty and oppression, and
our best hopes go out for the full success of their de
termined contest for liberty."
The Populist national convention declared: "We
tender to the patriotic people of Cuba our deepest
sympathy in their heroic struggle lor political free
dom and independence, and we believe the time has
come when the United States, the great republic of
the world, should recognize that Cuba is. and of right
ought to be, a free and independent state."
The Democratic, platform said: "We extend our
sympathy to the people of Cuba in their heroic strug
gle for liberty and independence."
Those expressions represented the sentiments of the
whole people. They were uttered in a spirit of devo
tion to our own institutions, and in a certain sense
were boastful of our own position as the citizens of
a country in which Government derives its just
powers from the consent of the governed. The very
language of some of those platform pledges was
taken from the heroic declaration of Thomas Paine
when he ended the intercession of the colonies at the
feet of George 111 by that bold speech, 'These colo
nies are. and of right ought to be, free and indepen
dent States."
There is no room for disputing that the American
people intended those platform pledges to apply
everywhere, in the East Indies as weir as in the West,
and any proposition to limit their application to one
place and apply the monarchical principles of impe
rialism in another place would have, been hotly re
sisted and beaten in any of the three national con
ventions.
Surely it is a startling fact that our imperialists are
now daily denouncing the spirit of those platforms
as "treason" and those who adhere to those pledges
as ''traitors."
We have preferred to abide by the American policy
and stand for that highest Americanism which ani
mated all political parties in 1896. If to stand fast
in that Spirit and purpose is treason^ by what term
can we characterize the expressions of the im
perialists?
One of them declares the Federal constitution out
grown and no longer worth discussing. Mr. Reid,
one of the negotiators of the Paris treaty, says: "It
is time to begin teaching the American people the
absurdity of. that clause in the Declaration of Inde
pendence which derives all the just powers of govern
ment from the consent of the governed." The Rev.
Dr. P. S. Benson says: "As to that hallowed docu
ment that declares that all governments derive their
just powers from the consent of the governed/if that
is to be literally construed, there never was a greater
lie palmed of! by the devil upon a credulous world."
The list of these imperialistic expressions can .be in
definitely extended. The form varies but little. Their
spirit is one of active revolt against -the -restraints
and limitations of the Federal constitution and of
most contemptuous repudiation of the principles of
the Declaration of Independence. One is justly
amazed at the large number of men who take this
position. They ask the- country to indorse them, to
affirm their views, and they denounce opposition as
unpatriotic and treasonable. All this brings in- sight
a very disquieting situation. If the patriotic "declara
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL,, SATURDAY, MAY 20, 1899. '
tions of the three political parties in 1896 are patriotic
no longer, and if government by consent of the gov
erned is a devilish lie, when the men who hold these
views achieve power the republic will have reversed
itself. Government will then be held to derive its
powers.: from the will of those who govern, and self
government will have perished from the earth. X *
Though we stood alone we propose to deny now
j and for all time the thesis of imperialism. We deny
j that the constitution is obsolete and the Declaration
iof Independence a lie. We deny that what was pa
j triotic three years ago is unpatriotic now, and we
( warn those who manage parties to avoid identifica
tion with these imperialist denunciations of Ameri
i can principles.
THE SPIRIT OF TRUE CITIZENSHIP
UNDER the title of "Civic Pride" Herbert E.
Law contributes to the Merchants' Association
Review a notably strong article on the nature
of the public spirit necessary to make San Francisco
great and prosperous. "Civic pride," he says, "seems
to be the one thing lacking among our business men
and newspaper men in many instances." His argu
ment is that we scold too much, that harping on evils
does not cure them. On the contrary, such self
denunciations do harm, for they are scattered far and
wide and give the city a bad reputation abroad. He
says:
"What we should do is to talk about the city, its
prospects and its possibilities. We want to dream
about them. We want to read about them. We
want to have them constantly before us and to tell of
them so firmly, so often and so well, that we. shall
make converts. We want to gather people to us, and,
with them, work out a great destiny for the city. As
to the disadvantages and the difficulties we have here,
there has never been a city of any note in the United
States that has had as few as San Francisco. Stop
and think for a moment of the tremendous disadvan
tage under which Chicago labored. It is within the
memory of man when the best part of Chicago could
not be given away. Cities, like other things, are what
people make them. We have every reason to be
enthusiastic over the future of San Francisco. It is
destined to be the center of the greatest traffic of all
the commercial cities of the Union."
There is much in the policy proposed that is attrac
â¢ tive, and no doubt there is considerable wisdom un
derlying it. It is well that a people should not infre
quently take notice and account of their advantages
and their excellencies and make them known to
others. Optimism not infrequently stimulates energy
, and encourages industry to greater efforts. It is al
ways productive of cheerfulness, and that in itself goes
a long way toward advancing the fortunes cither of
individuals or of communities, for it not only
strengthens the endeavors of the worker, but it wins
friends.
Nevertheless Mr. Law is hardly justified in treating
those who point out the evils of the city as persons
| lacking in civic pride or patriotism. An abuse that
'is never denounced will never be cured. Nor is it
correct to assume that the denunciations of such of-
fenses injure us abroad. Ship-owners : and sea-
tains, for example, do not need us to tell them
about the shipping facilities of San Francisco and of
the port charges. They are well aware of all our de-
fects. Saying nothing about them will not cure them,
j and, Mr. Law to the contrary notwithstanding*, harp-
ing on them has a tendency to do so. .We are in-
deed reforming by degrees the evils among us, and
may eventually remove them all. but we will never
do so by any process of boasting of the city or brag
: ging of ourselves.
THE DREYFUS CASE.
IT is now expected that the Dreyfus case will be
decided by the French Court of Cassation early in
June. There are few disinterested men who have
j followed the investigation without being led to a be-
lief in his innocence. It is demonstratively proved
j that his. conviction was procured by shameless frauds,'
: and that his protracted incarceration on Devils Island
I is a reproach to the republic of France.
It is useless now critically to examine the addi
tional evidence that has been received and published.
Much of it is cumulative. As a whole it is so con
clusive that it cannot be overborne by any combi
nation of military and political influence. The find
nation of military and political influence. The find
ing that Dreyfus was guilty must certainly be quashed,
and it does not appear that any retrial can be justified.
A singular phase of this extraordinary case is re-
vealed by a recent publication in which Canon Delas-
sus of Cambrai, in France, complained that Dreyfus
had received moral support in the United States, for
which he held the American Catholic church respon
sible. France is nominally a republic, and yet this
learned Frenchman is totally unable to comprehend
the formation and the expression of public opinion in
a country where there is a complete separation be-
tween church and state, and where, by constitutional
elimination, bigotry and intolerance are practically
impossible. Millions of Americans of all parties and
creeds, Catholic and Protestant alike, have considered
1 the Dreyfus case and have believed that he was a vie-
tim of fraud and of military oppression. They live
under a form of government limited to secular ob-
jects, under which man is invested with inalienable
rights, and among them that of being presumed to be
innocent of crime until proved to be guilty by reliable
and competent testimony. In the case of Dreyfus
they saw that he had been made a scapegoat by
French officers connected with the general staff of
the army, and that, by fabricated evidence, he had
been subjected to cruel and undeserved punishment.
The question of his nationality or his religion was
not even an element in their consideration of. the
facts. Equality before the law, fair and open trial,
freedom from concocted persecutions, judgment rest*
ing upon truth and upon principle, arc the preroga
tives of every human being in the United States, citi-
zen or alien, barbarian or civilized, Jew or Gentile,
i Pagan or Christian.
American citizenship, therefore, without distinction
of race, party or creed, resents tyranny and injustice,
no matter what the pretexts or the sophistries in-
volved to justify them. It inquires only into the case
itself, when its intelligence and its manhood are en-
listed by such conditions as surrounded the trial and
the exile of Dreyfus. It condemns persecutions in
America, in Russia, in Germany, in Ireland, in
France â wherever humanity exists.
The attack of Canon Dclassus upon the American
Catholic church, using the phrase in which >'. he
groups together our Catholic fellow citizens, was not
only unwarrantable but evidently grew out of his
total inability to separate questions of government
from questions of race or of religion. It was simi
lar to the virulent criticisms by Dr. Zahn of Arch-
bishop Ireland, whose patriotism has been so deeply
| recognized in this country, who has been so de
; servedly and so signally honored by Pope Leo XIII,
and whose recent address upon Joan of Arc was
| among the notable productions of the century. The
; indignation of Washington churchmen, so forcibly
expressed, will be shared by American citizens gen-
erally, who regard the separation of church and state
as a fundamental necessity and who believe that po-
I litical liberty is best subserved by religious neutrality.

The Attorney for the Corporation
The Attorney for the Corporation
Backs Up His Contention With
a Clear Explanation of
the Law.
The controversy between the Board
of Supervisors and the Spring Valley
Water Company over the payment of
the company's bill for water furnished
the city during the month of March
has been finally settled and the com
pany will get the full amount of its
claim. It was alleged that during the
past five years the water company had
charged foe water furnished the whole
of Alta Plaza, when, as a matter of
fact, only half of the plaza has been
under cultivation. It was claimed that
these overcharges amounted to $6110 92,
and an attempt Was. made to deduct
this amount from the March bill.
The matter was referred to the Com
mittee on Water and Water Supply for
investigation and was taken up for con
sideration at the meeting of the com
mittee held yesterday morning. Edward
Deady, ex-gardener of Alta Plaza, was
called as a witness and testified that
during the years 1897 and 1898 no-water
was used on the unimproved portion
of the park. He admitted that mains
and pipes were in place and the water
ready for use, but could not remember
but orfe or two instances when it had
been used. It developed, however, that
in the latter part of 1894 the city let
a contract for the grading and improv
ing of that portion of the plaza now in
dispute and that water was used by
tho contractor for a year thereafter.
George E. Booker, chief clerk for the?
water company, said water had been
us* on the disputed portion of the
plaza during the greater portion of
the period covered by the alleged over
charges. He also said that not until
the park was under cultivation did the
company put in its bill to the city. He
stated that it was a general rule with
him never to make any charge until
a place as under cultivation.
Supervisor Bylngton asked Mr.
Booker if he could tell -what amount
of water had been used In Alta Plaza
from October, 1594, to the present time,
but Mr. Booker was unable to enlight
en him. He did say he had seen the
water used, but added that he did not
think the park was kept up in very
good condition.
The chief clerk informed the com
mittee that the bills of his company
had frequently been voluntarily re
duced. He said the water company had
charged the city at a low rate so as to
keep within the one-twelfth act, as it
could not afford to come before the
board and have its bills tied up, as
such a course would result in the com
pany losing a part or even the whole
of the amount of its bills. He also
said the company was making Its
charges less than they should be if the
legal rates were charged in every in
stance.
Mr. Booker testified that he lived in
the vicinity of Alta Plaza and watched
its progress. He was positive the water
had been used.
At the conclusion of Chief Clerk
Booker's statement Attorney Kellogg,
representing the water company, said.
"The. evidence shows conclusively
that water was supplied to the entire
Alta Plaza Park In 1894 and that for
nearly a year- it was continuously used
by the city in irrigating that park.
Afterward the city used the water on
tho disputed half of the park and never
from the time water was turned on
by the company until the month of
March. 1899, did the city ever- orally
or in writing notify the company that
it was not using or did not desire to
use the water. And the company did
not know until March of this year that
the city desired to discontinue Its use.
"Even then" the authorities desired
the water to remain on the unused por
tion of the park, but requested that the
amount be deducted from their bills.
This was immediately done and has
since been done.
"All the bills from 1894 to March, 1899,
were audited and approved by the
proper committee of the board; and by
the board and the city officers, and
paid. This was done after the very
question now under discussion had been
thoroughly investigated by the preced
ing board, acting under the advice of
City and County Attorney Creswell,
which I will' read to you. - It is as fol
lows:
Liability of city and county to be.
charged for water when* notice to dis-
continue supply has not been given.
February 9, 1895.
To the Board of Supervisors: I am
in receipt of the communication of the
joint committee on judiciary and
finance of the board dated January
29, 1895, asking my opinion of the lia
bility of the city and county for the
charge of $723 50 of the Spring Valley :''
Water Works for water supplied In
the month of December, 1894, for pub-
lie squares and parks. The charge
was made under resolution No. 10,049
(third series!, adopted by the Board of
Supervisors March .12, 1894, which is
the present water ordinance.
I do not of my own knowledge know
whether or not water belonging to the
Spring Valley Water Works had been
prior ; to December, 1894, supplied by
that company to the city a,nd county
for the irrigation of the public squares
and parks by means of pipes or other-
wise extending into the same, and if
so, whether the -water was in Decem
ber, 1894, ready for the use of the city
and county for that purpose at ' the
pleasure of- its employe,-.
I will assume, however, that such is
the case, and that prior to December
1894. the city and county had been us-
ing the same.
There is a general rule of law. sup-
ported by decisions too numerous to
cite, that "a sale is perfected by de
'-. livery when . the property is so situ-
ated that the purchaser is entitled to
and can rightfully take possession of
it at his pleasure."
Benjamin on Sales, 3d Am. Ed., sec
, 679, cases cited. .: . ...
Means vs. Williamson, 37 Me. 556
If. then, according to this rule of
law, the water had been delivered by]
the Spring Valley Water Works In
December. 1894, upon the public
squares and parks for the use of the
city and county for irrigation, and was
so : situated that the city and county
was entitled to and could < rightfully
. take possession of it at pleasure, then
. the sale of it to the city and county â 
was complete, and the cost . of the
same at the established rates Is a
legal charge against the city and
county. '-â¢-â ' .*-.'
- No. 10,049 is the present
water ordinance of the Board of Su
pervisors.
Section 14 of this resolution requires
in effect, that all consumers shall give
; notification to the company in writing
to cut off or dicontinue the water sud-
ply upon the premises of the consum
er, when there Is an intention to dis-
continue the use thereof, "after
â which," in the language* of the section,
"no charge shall be made for water
for said premises until the use of wa
ter is resumed." ."â  â  _,
If this section refers to the muni-
cipal corporation of the city and coun
ty of San Francisco, as well as to all
private corporations and natural per-
sons, then, * before a charge for the
water in December, 1894, could be de-
feated, it would have been necessary
for the city and county to have given
the company the notice therein re-
quired. - , ,
In this ordinance is found frequent
mention of the City and County of San
Francisco. .V , .
For instance, the first sentence pro-
vides, among other things, for monthly
rates or compensation to be collected
hv any person, companay or corpora-
tion engaged in the business of sup-
plying water * * â¢ for muni-
cipal uses, and for all public purposes
'of the city and county of San l' ran-
cisco; and section 11 provides the rates
of compensation to be collected for
water supplied by and through hy
drants to the city and county of San
Francisco; and section 13 provides that .
all water rates, except meter rates
and city and county rates, are due and
payable monthly in advance, and that
meter and city and county rates are
due and payable at the end of each
month. , ._.â_!_
These citations show conclusively
that the Board of Supervisors in pass-
ing resolution No. 10,049, recognized
that the municipal corporation 01 the
city and county of San Francico would
be a purchaser and consumer or water
under that ordinance. ââ¢___ â¢
Section 14. as I have shown, requires
notice of discontinuance to be given
by consumers. It applies to all con-
sumers alike; it does not say, and
there can be no reason why it should
have said, that natural persons who
were consumers of water should M
burdened with this requirement while
public and private corporations should
not be. . . IC-,',
The only foundation for an argu
ment that this section does not apply
to public and private corporations as
well as natural persons is the use
therein of the personal pronoun his
The use of this word does not limit
the application of this section to
natural persons. Even had the word
"person" been used instead of the
word "consumer." the section would
have applied to public and private cor
j porations as well as to natural per-
sons. . â _,"_''â '"_ ._
It has been repeatedly held by the
courts of last resort in the United
' States that "when persons are men-
tioned in a statute corporations are in-
eluded if they fall within the general
reason and design of the statute.
(Angell and Ames on Corporations,
sees. 6, 193, 265, 271, 407 a. 441. 458.)
The application of the "person
to corporations Is recognized by the
Codes of California, sec. 14, Civil Code.
I am therefore of the opinion that if
the Spring Valley Water Works sup-
plied water to the city and county so
that the same could be used for the-
irrigation of the public squares and
parks at the pleasure of the employe's
of the city and county entrusted with
that duty, and that the city and
county gn,ve no notice to discontinue
the use of the water for the month
of December, 1896, the city and county
is legally liable for water for that
month at the rate established by Res-
olution No. 10,049 C^lrdCserlesV. â¢
HARRY T. CRESWELL,
Attorney and Counselor.
"This doctrine," Mr. Kollogg con
tinued, "has been maintained by every
court where the question has arisen
and it has been universally recognized
by the municipal authorities here since
it was enunciated by Mr. Creswell. It
also is apparent from the testimony of
the gardener as well as Mr. Booker
that the city has used the water as
occasion required. It is therefore not
legal nor equitable in view of the doc
trines stated in the opinion and in view
of the facts, that the present board
should attempt to review acts of their
predecessors which had been not only
adjudicated but completed in accord
ance -with the doctrines of the board.
"Payments have been made and ac
cepted and receipts passed in full
knowledge of the existing circumstan
ces. There was, therefore, no error
whatever from any point of view in the
bills -which had been presented and
paid and no right vested in this present
board to review such acts of their pre
decessors as had become functus officio.
When it was admitted, as it must be,
that the claims of the company had
been properly presented and paid and
that they were just and proper, I stand
ready, not as a matter of right upon
the part of the municipality, but for the
sake of avoiding further disputes and
delays and for the purpose of closing
up the business of the fiscal year in
due time, to deduct from the June bill
the amount in dispute. I do this be
cause there would be a deficiency in
the general fund and because I desire
no further disputes or delays In the
payment of the already accrued hills
and the bills to accrue between now
and the fiscal year. - It must be dis
tinctly understood, however, that this
was not done as an admission of any
right on the part of the city, but with
a constant denial of that right and the
assertion fully sustained by the facts
and the law, that all the bills of the
company have been correct and legal."
The committee deemed this offer to
be a fair one and so accepted it. A
recommendation will be made to the
board on Monday that {he full amount
of the disputed claim be paid.

gentleman accompanies a lady to a thea
ter he should not leave his seat until it is
time to rise and escort the lady home. At
the opera, when there are long waits be
tween acts, the gentleman can ask the
lady to promenade, but if she declines it
is his duty to remain seated with her and
engage her in pleasant conversation.

PRIESTS REFUSE
TO ADMINISTER
A BIG BEQUEST
A Hundred Thousand
Rejected.
â â¦ â
THE ARCHBISHOP STEPS IN
â â¦ â
WANTS THE DONAHUE LIBRARY
, FOR YOUNG MEN.
Â«
An Amicable Contention at Law' In-
An Amicable Contention at Law' In-
volving an. Intricate Point and
a Large Sum of
Money.
It is not every day that people can b^
found with courage enough to refuse Â«_f
legacy of 1100,000, no matter how arduous
the conditions attached. Still there are
some In this city, the trustees of St. Ig
natius College being among the number.
Under the will of the late Mrs. Annie
Donahue, widow of Peter Donahue, there
was left to them the sum of $100,000 to be
used in founding ahd supporting for the
benefit of Catholic young men a library,
to be called in memory of her husband
the Donahue library. At a recent meet
ing of the college trustees it was de
cided to reject the bequest for the reason
that under its terms no provision was
made for the payment of salaries and
other incidental expenses of the library
which the trustees did not feel they could
undertake.
Archbishop Riordan, who naturally took
a great deal of interest in such an insti
tution, seeing that it was in his arch
diocese that the benefits of the library
would be most generally felt, learned with
regret of the rejection of the legacy. He
decided if possible that the institution
should be founded, and to that end he
has asked the courts to substitute him
self as trustee, in plage of those named .
In the bequest, signifying his willingness
to carry out Its provisions and erect and
maintain the libracy for Catholic young
men. . â¢ ,
The application has just been presented
in the Superior Court by the Attorney
General on the relation of the Arch
bishop. It will be heard next Friday be
fore Superior Judge Troutt. when ex-At
torney General Fitzgerald and Robert Y.
Havne will appear in support of the ap
plication, and John Garber and Barclay
Henley, representing the heirs and de-^jA
visees under the will, will resist it.
This resistance of the heirs and de
visees to the application of the Arch
bishop is understood to be entirely
friendly and undertaken only with a de
sire to have the matter forever adjusted
by the court. Taking this position, the
heirs contend that ft is apparent from
the face of the will that Mrs. Donahue
appointed the college trustees by reason
of the personal trust and confidence she
had in their efficiency as educators and
upon their known character and ability
to administer this kind of a trust.
The Attorney General takes the ground
that the Archbishop, having been ap
pointed a trustee for the distribution of
1100.000 in charity under another provision
of the will, and being the official resi
dent head of all things Catholic in the
State; is naturally the trustee to be se
lected to carry out the will of the de
ceased in repaid to a Catholic library
after the rejection of the office by the
trustees first appointed.
The question at issue is one of consid
erable interest to attorneys, involving as
it uoes the principle of cy pres (law
French), meaning "as near as" â that is,
in this case to carry out as closely as pos
sible the intention of the testatrix. This
intention, .the relator holds, was for the
founding of a library alone, the appoint
ment of trustees being merely an inci
dental matter.

THE Endeavorers are now in active
session. Work is the keynote of
the active Christians, and for the
moment ill hospitable intentions
are put aside and the business of
the convention is being strictly ad
hered to. "After the routine of the three
days' session pleasure will be In order,
and for next week a series of delights has
been carefully planned.
The steamer Piedmont has been char
tered for Sunday and the Endeavorers
will take a trip on the bay. Tuesday will
be given over to scaling the heights of
grim old ramalpais. Santa Cruz and the
big trees will fill in the pleasures of
Wednesday, and on Thursday a visit to
Santa Rosa will be in order. Friday will
bring the festivities to a close with a
"basket lunch" at Haywards.
The work of the Endeavorer begins
early. At a quarter before R Rev. XV. C.
Sherman of the Fourteenth-street Pres
byterian Church at Sacramento opened
to-day's session with "The Quiet Morning
Hour"." an Impressive service of song,
prayer and Scripture res ling. R. W.
Venn of Santa Paula in a "Chalk Talk
followed. A number of hymns were then
sung under tho leadership of E. Mere
dith and Rev. H. M. Tenny of the First
Congregational Church of San Jose read
a Scripture lesson. _"'._,

After the committees were disposed of
the State officers and superintendents
gave their "Last Year's Reflections." All
the reflections showed the result of much
mature thought and a spirit imbued with
tie- nobleness of the cause. Belle _' Na
son's paper on "Junior and Intermediate
vÂ»ork pointed out the great opportuni
ties for the laborer in this- Held. E. C
Gilberts reflection on "Commercial and
Railroad Men" was Bet forth In rhyme.
but no .acta were sacrifice, at the ex-
pense of tho music of his 'verse. E. 11.
' Baker's paper, "The Quiet Hour," was an
earnest plea for the prayer that is made
in the closet and the good deeds done In
secret. A. XV. Bell followed with "Good
Literature" and made an earnest plea for "
the spread of the highest and best 'read-
; ing. L. T. Hatfield, in his reflection on
"Temperance and Citizenship." urged all
1 to "carry their church principles to the
polls."
Lester B. Smith, State chairman on
transportation, who. according to Presi-
dent -Lowell, "is in with the Southern
, Pacific, but does not wear the collar,"
1 gave a graphic description of how he got
the Endeavorers to Oakland. Mr. Smith
explained that all credit was not duo to
' himself only, but to the wideawake En-
deavorers all over the State.
State Treasurer J. A. Mil. presented
his yearly report. It showed cash on

pledges and collections, $107723; sale of
song hooks. $47 91. Total, 51131 9Â«. Dis

General Secretary Francis W. Reid said
there were three kinds of black lies.
white lies and statistics. lie read some
! of the latter and made them both inter-
esting and Instructive, touching upon the
whole with a dash of contagious humor.
President Lowell was the last officer on
' the morning programme, and he expressed
his, gratitude to the Endeavorers for their
kindness to him and for their spirit of
harmony and co-operation.
Reports were received from the county
i unions of Shasta. San Joaquin, Solano.
Siskiyou, Sacramento. Orange, Santa
Cruz. Sonoma, Napa and San Francisco.
Mrs. Edmund Taylor of Oakland, the

perience with sailors and declared that
Jack Is a big-hearted, frank fellow, with

Mizi.ah benediction. _.___ _ , _.
When the Rev. J. H. McCollough of the
Christian church of Irvington opened .the.
afternoon session with a prayer there
was scarcely a vacant seat in the great
assembly hall. The "Question Box, con-
ducted by Rev. George C. Adams of the
THE SAN FltAlS CISCO CAM.. SATURDAY, MAY 20, 1899.
First Congregational Church, San Fran
cico, brought comfort to -many restless
spirits. Dr. Adams answered the many
puzzling, perplexing questions aptly and
satisfactorily.
Dr. Clark, the reverend founder of the
order, followed, speaking on "The Quiet
Hour." Dr. Clark in convincing phrase
spoke of the "fifteen minutes alone with
God" and dilated' upon the wonderful
blessing of communing with the Maker.
W. G. Alexander's address. "What Aye
Owe to Our Intermediates and Juniors,
was filled with noble thoughts and con
tained an earnest plea to save the "Jun
ior" from drifting away. "Many Jun
iors," Mr. Alexander explained, "when
they reach the years between 14 and 20
feel they are too old for Juniors and too
Weak to "grapple with the earnest pur
poses of the Seniors. We must keep them
from drifting from out the circle of good
influences, and that Is the especial work
of the Intermediates."
Tho Chinese children from the San
Francisco mission interrupted for a pleas
ant moment the solemnity of the proceed
ings, and in childish treble sang hymns of
praise and patriotic songs.
Rev. F. B. Cherlngton of the Plymouth
Congregational Church told of the "Mis
sion of Christian Literature," and happily
commended the fact that the horrible,
realistic fin-de-siecle literature was
doomed to perish and the good works,
such as "Pilgrim's Progress" and the
works of Thomas a Kempis destined to
live forever.
After a prayer and benediction by Rev.
V. L. Fulton of the First United Presby
terian Church of Alameda the Endeavor
ers separated into small gatherings to tit
tend the various following "open parlia
ments": " -..::â :â <

The evening session of the convention
was largely devotional. Like last night
the big hall was packed, and the police
earned many unkind looks for its enforce
ment of the ordinance' providing that all
aisles and exits be kept clear. Rev. _. A.
Woods, D.D., of the First Baptist Church
of San Francisco, delivered a short ser
mon, dealing with the responsibilities of
Christian Endeavorers.
Rev. "Father" Clark, whose presence Is
always the signal for a salute, spoke from
the subject "Detroit, '99." He reminded
the convention that the national gather
ing of the society was to be held in De
troit in July, and that California send as
large a delegation as possible, as all the
States were anxious to return the gener
osity shown by California in '97 at the
San Francisco convention. The speaker
dwelt at length upon the need of En
deavorers abiding by the pledge required
of them to do their whole duty to God
and man. He advised them not to be car
ried away by enthusiasm and ostentation,
but to follow closely the vocation to
which they were called.
Rev. C. Pierce of Los Angeles dealt with
the subject ''Giving' to God." and "Oppor
tunity" was the theme of Rev. G. C. Ad
ams of San Francisco. The latter speaker
said that the true creed of all Christian
Endeavor was embraced in his text. The
opportunity was at hand, the 'fields are
white unto harvest and the world looks
to the Endeavorers to gather In the
sheaves. *
The session was brought to a close with
a prayer and benediction by Rev. J. c.
Edwards of the Los Angeles African M.
E. church. WlM'i
BUNKER HILL DAY.
Preparations for a Grand Celebration
Being Made.
At the regular meeting of the executive
committee of arrangements for the cele
bration of the anniversary of the battle
of Bunker Mill on June 17 next at Sun
set Park, letters were read from Mayor
Phelan accepting the Invitation to be
present and promising that flags* should
bo raised on all the public buildings of
the city; from Mrs. Henry Gibbons, ac
cepting the invitation, to Puerto del Oro
Chapter of Daughters of the American
Revolution, and from Charles S. Greene,
editor of the Overland, accepting the in
vitation ir. be poet of the day.
The transportation committee reported
that extraordinary precautions would be
taken to protect the number of excur
sionists who would visit Sunset Park.
The order of exercises, as completed is:
Prayer by the chaplain of the day. Rev.
T. J. I.acey; remarks by the president of
the day. William G. Badger; oration by
Frank McGowan; original poem by
Charles S. Greene, the poet of the day;
Bong, '.'The Sword of Bunker .Hill," by
bert Duncan; address by Joaquin Mil-
Childreo of the Chinese Missior) in
Prayer and Praise.
ler; singing by the Columbia Quartet;
"The Star Spangjed Banner." by Miss
Millie Flynn; "Marseillaise Hymn," by
Miss Adelaide Roddy, and "America," by
the audience.
This programme will occupy but an
hour of th,. time, and the remainder of
the day can be devoted to dancing, games
and walks In the beautiful grounds of the
park. ; - -.-
Excursion to Ukiah.
The California Northwestern Railway
will run an excursion to, Ukiah Sunday,
May .1. The fare will be only $2 for the
round trip. The time of departure from
Tiburon ferry, foot of Market street, will
be 8:30 a, m., and from : Ukiah on ', the
return 5 o'clock p. m.,' giving a lay over
of about four and a half hours. On this
trip the California Northwestern Railway
will use its solid train of new passenger
coaches just received from the East. Most
modern in their -design and elegantly
equipped;, they possess all 'tire latest- Im
provements for comfort and safety.., â¢

Governor Gage is expected in San Fran- i
cisco to-day to attend the cornerstone ,
ceremonies at the College of Physicians ;
and Surgeons. Hearing that he had ac
cepted an Invitation to meet the physi
cians many of the politicians surmised !
that the appointments to the Board of
Health -would either be announced or i
plainly indicated before the Governor left I
the city for Los Angeles. In expectation
of the Governor's arrival from Sacra- !
mento many of the applicants for official I
favor went to the Palace court last even- '
ing. but they did not meet the executive.
There are many subordinate positions !
In tho field on inspection connected with ;
the Health Department or this city, hence j
unusual interest is manifested. The Gov- j
ernor is blamed for delay in naming the â 
new board, but his friends say that Gov- \
ernor Budd did not appoint the Health
Board until in the month of June.
An officeholder who Is supposed to he ln
the confidence of Burns and McLaughlin
expressed the opinion last night atÂ» the
Palace that DO one but Gage had the
slightest knowledge as to the accepted
Board of Health slate. A month ago the
insiders believed that the selections had
been finally made, but since the extreme
pressure had been brought to bear on the
Governor to Induce him to recognize an
active local element of the party that was
not considered when the original slate
was made.
It is now understood that members of
the executive committee of the â¢ Repub- ;
lican State Central Committee, who were
not known in the Senatorial contest at
Sacramento as active supporters of D. M. j
Burns, will make no further suggestions
to Governor Gage concerning the distri- i
bution of patronage.
The appointments made by the Governor
since the conference, at the Palace Hotel, :
which was called ostensibly to strengthen
and harmonize the party, convince all j
who were not In the Burns crowd that it
la utterly useless to make any suggestions
to Gage.
Another chapter in the record of Burns*
defalcation in office is closed. A. L. Hart,
who defended D. M. Burns In the courts
of Yolo and Sacramento, where embez
zlement proceedings were instituted,
sought subsequently to collect $2">,000 for
legal services rendered the accused. D.
M. Burns paid (2000 on account, but re
fused to pay the full amount of Hart's
fee.
When the Senatorial contest opened at
Sacramento the lawyer fancied that the
time was opportune for demanding final
settlement. The ex-Secretary of State
saw at once . that refusal to entertain
propositions might reopen the old scan- ;
dal and bring to light some phases of
the embezzlement of public funds which
would affect his candidacy for the United
States Senate. ln order, therefore, to
suppress scandal, arbitration was sug- j
gested. It appears that Burns and Hart
agreed on Colonel J. B. Wright of the
Southern Pacific Company as arbitrator. I
In due time Colonel Wright submitted a
report, in which he decided that D. M. '
Burns should pay A. 1.. Hart $1500. This J
sum, which Burns has now 'paid, added
to a previous payment of 0000, makes
Mart's fee for defending Burns $3500. In
accordant with the arbitrator's report,
the proceeding, in the Superior Court to i
obtain judgment against Burns have
been dismissed.

A LOST RING RESTORED.
Attorney Hart Secures a Sparkler He
. Claims Was Taken From Him
by Force.
The mystery surrounding the loss of ex-
Attorney General A. L. Hart's diamond
ring has not been cleared up, but the ;
missing jewel has been recovered. It was
returned to General Hart yesterday by
Chief of Police Fletcher of Oakland. The
promised reward of $100 will be paid At
torney Hugh Aldrich.
The manner in which General Hart was
separated' from the ring, which was a
present from his wife, may never be
known. According to his story he was
drugged with knock-out drops while jour- I
neying from Sacramento to this city, j
While under the influence of some drug, i
he claims, the ring was removed from his
finger forcibly. He did not miss it until
he reached the end of his journey
He advertised at once, -offering' a re- j
ward of .100 for the return of his property !
Upon the appearance of the advertisement !
Attorney Aldrich answered it. He claimed
that his client", a reputable resident of '
Oakland, found the ring as he- was leav
ing the train at Sixteenth street and com
missioned him to return it upon payment '
of the proffered reward. The attorneys
could not agree for some time as to the
method to be pursued in returning the
ring. They finally settled their differences
and the property was returned to its
rightful owner.

PLAYED WITH MATCHES
AND BURNED TO DEATH
FATE OF FIVE-YEAR-OLD GRACE
THEUNER.
Help Came Quickly, but Too Late to
Save the Childâ She Died
in a Few Hours.
Grace, the five-year-old daughter of
Henry Theuner of ISO Parker avenue was
badly burned yesterday noon while play
ing with matches. She died at 7 o'clock
last night.
According to the statement of the
mother, the little one had a strange fancy
for matches and whenever the parental
eye was turned away she would steal a
bunch and in some secluded corner she
would amuse herself by striking them
and watching them burn.
Just before the noon hour yesterday,
while Mrs. Theuner was preparing lunch
eon, little Grace secured a bunch of
matches and toddled over to a vacant lot
opposite the house, where she proceeded
with her favorite amusement. Soon
after the mother was startled by the
agonizing shrieks of her child. Rushing
to the front door she found little Grace
making her way toward the house with
her clothing all ablaze. The mother
rushed into the house for blankets, but
her 11-year-old son Willie was quicker.
He secured a bucket of water and with
it extinguished the flames.
The child was carried into the house
and Drs. Neimler and Cleary were caller*
in. They found the child was past hu
man aid*. The- whole lower portion of
her body was charred, the flesh almost
dropping from the wounds. The doctors
applied temporary remedies and recom
mended that the little one be removed to
the Children's Hospital. This was done.
The physicians there bandaged the body
more thoroughly, but all the time they
could see that the little life was fast eb
bing away. After a few hours' suffering
little Grace breathed her last.
After the accident there was a rumor
to the effect that the child's clothing was
set on fire in play by another little girl.
Diligent inquiry showed that there was
no truth in the statement.
Circus in Town.
An Indoor circus and a Swiss Alpine
i wrestling tournament will be the draw
ing card at Glen Park to-morrow (Sun
i day). Under the auspices of the Swiss
Athletic Club a number of the club
members will contest for prizes, com
'â  mencing at 12 m. on the recreation
grounds in front of the heater.
The "circus in the barn" will be made
lup of the following attractions: Bare
back trick riding, hurdle racing, Olym
pian riding, steeplechasing, trained ani
mals, Japanese acrobats, umbrella toss-
ing, trapeze performance, tight-rope
wonders, feats of strength, equilibrists,
tumblers and funny clowns.
A Supposed Tool Thief.
John Donovan was locked in the tanks
yesterday by Detectives Ryan and
ODea, who placed against" him. a charge
of burglary. He is accused of stealing
several hundred dollars' worth .of iron
and steel workers* tools.
Donovan is supposed to be the man
who broke into the Murphy-Grant build
ing on McAllister and Jones streets a
few days ago and stole all the. tools ne
could carry away from the storeroom.
Some men from the Ralston Iron Works
had been putting up a fire escape, and he
left them with hardly a tool to work
with. â¢ Some men doing similar work on
the Columbian building were treated to
a similar visit a few days before. Most
of these tools have been recovered.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver unchanged. 'Yt's
Wheat firm. Barley advancing rapidly.
Oats and Corn firm.
Rye remains dull.
Hay shows more strength. â¢
Middlings advanced. ' Bran firm.
Rolled Barley higher.
Beans and Seeds unchanged.
Linseed Oil and Lucol marked up.
New Potatoes coming in freely.
Cheese lower again.
Butter and Eggs steady.
Poultry stands about the same. /
High prices reported for Apricots and Pears.
Oregon fruit crop deficient.
Citrus fruits quiet.
Provisions unchanged.
Hogs slightly off.
Other Meats unchanged.
Grain Bags weaker.
EXPORTS OF WINE.
EXPORTS OF WINE.
Exports of Wine from this port by sea dur
ing the first four months of the year wore 1,
--844,500 gallons and 2538 cases, against 1,83.,800
gallons and 2886 cases for the same time In
-1893. ;
'OREGON CROP PROSPECTS.
OREGON CROP PROSPECTS.
B. S. Pague, Government Section Director for j
Oregon, Department of Agriculture, gives an I
unfavorable outlook for the fruit and grain j
crops in Oregon this year. The Prune crop j
will be very short, and ln some districts a fail- j
ure. The trees blossomed heavily, but when
the fruit began 'to set it dropped off. This is j
precisely what occurred in California. The j
same condition applies to ' Peaches. Apricots I
and Cherries. Pears and Apples promise a good |
crop. In conclusion, the report says:
"The reports from all sections of the State
do not indicate that favorable condition of
products and live stock which is usual. It must .
be acknowledged that current weather condi- ]
tions are not favorable to good crops, and .
present prospects are not encouraging. A
change to more seasonable weather will cor- j
rect much of that which Is now so adverse;
this Is true of grain and root crops. The fruit
crop, from present reports, will not be as large
as It was last year, and it may yet prove to
Ie poorest on record."

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. May 19.â The stock market was
abandoned almost wholly to professional trad
ers on the floor of the exchange. No outside
demand was apparent at any point ln the list.
Prices were marked up sharply at the open
ing on a demand from the shorts who sold
the specialties yesterday. Sugar, Brooklyn and i
the Flower group generally showed the larg
est gains, the demand being obviously from
the short interests. Anaconda mining showed
strength both In London and here, which was
well maintained* throughout the day, and the ;
tone of Manhattan was also firm without any j
published news to account for it. The open
ing demand from the shorts was soon satisfied
and prices slipped away, but without any pres
sure to sell. The dullness at the decline seemed
to Impress the professionals that the reaction
was over and they turned buyers again, mark- ,
ing prices up, especially in Sugar and Brook
lyn Transit. But it became quickly evident
that 'the bull interests which were active early ,
in the week were not prepared to resume oper
ations and the advance did not hold. The ab- :
sence of any demand for stocks caused the '
traders to turn bean again, and the market
sagged and drifted quite aimlessly for the bal
ance of the day. The short sales by the trad- i
ers were covered before the close, bringing
prices in a great many cases back to about
last night's level. London bought a few stocks i
In this market, but took no active part ow
ing to the pending holidays there.
The railroad bond market continued to show !
a large Investment demand, especially for re- 'â¢
cent reorganization issues. Total sales $4,437,- I
00('. United States new 4s advanced % ln the I
bid price.
The total sales of stocks to-day amounted to I
443.480 shares, including: American Steel and
Wire, SOOO; American Sugar Refining Company, !
56,800: American Tobacco. 7100; Anaconda, 11,
--300; Atchison preferred, 30,800; Brooklyn Rapid
Transit, 77.200: Brunswick Company, 4400; Cen- :

STOCK OPERATIONS ON WALL STREET.
NEW YORK. May 19. â Bradstreefs Re-
view of the New York stock market to-
morrow will say: Ex-Governor Flower
had been the most conspicuous bull leader f&r
the past two weeks, and the success which had
attended his operations in People's Gas, Rock
Island, New York Air Brake, Federal Steel,
Brooklyn Rapid Transit and ether leading
stocks had given an influence with the
speculative public unparalleled in the history
of the stock market. The unexpected news of
his death which awaited Wall street last i
Saturday morning seemed to many the signal i
for a decided break In the market. On the I
other hand. it was quickly recognized that the j
large financial Interests engaged ln the mar- |
ket would be sure to prevent any serious mis-
hap. This proved to be the case, and though j
at the opening on Saturday morning a- flood of j
short selling and liquidation was met which
carried People's Gas down from 119, the pre- '
ceding night's close, to Ml, Brooklyn Rapid j
Transit from 117% to 100 and Federal Steel from
61% to 50, with corresponding declines In not
only the Flower stocks, but ln other parts of
the list, there was almost instantly a sharp
rally. Brokers who are credited with acting
for the Standard Oil Interest, for Keene and
other large operators, had buying orders and
prices were advanced so that a large part of the
early declines were regained. The danger of
an outpouring of the long stocks held by com-
mission houses for their customers was thus
averted, and the liquidation, if one was neces
sary, became a question of time. Further as-
sistance was given to the market by the ap
pearance of an excellent bank statement on
, Saturday and by the assurance that the lead-
ing banks would, so far as it was proper for
them to do so, extend all needful accommoda
tion to stock market borrowers.- It also ap
peared that the late Mr. Flower had no large
speculative holdings, that the firm in which he
was a principal partner would continue with-
out change and that his associates were fully
convinced of the value â his properties. Be-
vond this it was recognized that the larger in-
terests .of the street having various Important
deals on hand, like the Amalgamated Copper
Company subscription or the financing of the
i Carnegie Steel Company, would not and could
not allow the market to become demoralized.
' Support from such quarters was plainly ap-
parent throughout the week. The Indisposition
of the general public to buy stocks and the ten-
dency to reduce long holdings was also no less
: marked. London, though at first obliged to
acquire stocks at the concessions, afterward
turned a seller and was credited with dispos-
â¢ ing of considerable holdings of Baltimore and
I Ohio securities and of Southern Pacific when
they exhibited a rising disposition. The mark-
ed strength of the bond market- and advance of
Government bond values, together with the rel
; ative steadiness of the dividend paying stock
list, was taken into account by the market, but
i on the other hand the granger shares seemed
heavy, a fact attributed to the continuance of
' unfavorable crop rumors. The support, which
has already been described, was in the earlier
part of the week largely concentrated in the
i Flower Blocks and the Industrials. Sugar. was
'â  made active and strong again and further re-
I coveries scored in People's Gas, Federal Steel,
Brooklyn Rapid Transit and their companions.
' The decision of Governor Roosevelt to call an
; extra session of the New York Legislature for
â¢ the purpose of amending the Ford franchise tax
I bill was another factor having a favorable ef
fect upon speculative sentiment. Nevertheless,
by Thursday a renewed reaction took place ln
the Flower stocks and industrials, while the
â  support seemed to shift to the grangers and
i the dividend . payers. On Friday the market
was generally dull and Irregular and showed
a disposition to sell off.
I BRADSTREET'S ON CONDITION OF TRADE
NEW YORK, May 19.â Bradstreefs of to-
morrow will says:
The feature of the trade situation this week
is the renewed strength of wheat and other
cereals, iron and steel and last, but not least.
raw wool, which has heretofore lagged behind
other staples in a discouraging way. The
, price-making features in cereals are not en-
tirely of encouraging character, being chiefly
; the less favorable reports as to the growing
crop West and Northwest and advances of in
' sect damage influencing Western markets
i toward a higher plane in the face of a dls-
couragingly small export business, caused in
' some degree by the continuance of the strike
'of grain-handlers at Buffalo, checking the
i movements of large quantities of grain to the
seaboard. The added strength in iron is based
Jargely upon heavy buying of plgiron in the
Central West, sympathetically influencing
prices at all markets. Reports are of old and
' new furnaces going Into blast, but these ad
' vices and the new plants projected to be built
' apparently do not reassure consumers, who are
beginning to place orders for the first part
of next year at present prices. Excitement ln
iron is not ' confined entirely to this country.
i however, as shown by a speculative advance at
leading British markets, but a sample of the
Irregularity caused by the immense current
I world's demand Is furnished in the case of for-
eigners seeking supplies in this country at the
same time that orders for rails have been
placed by American companies with the Eng-
; lish makers. Foreign wool markets continue
above American, heavy sales of Australian
i wools for re-export are made and heavy sup
j porters of .this class of wool are nearly ex-
hausted at' Boston. Aside from the exceptions
noted the tendency is toward steadiness ln
prices and most provisions, coffee and sugar
l among groceries, lead and copper among met-
I als and cotton and its various manufactures
' among textiles, are all reported unchanged on
i the week. Speculation in cotton lags, notwith-
standing good trade advices from home and
abroad, and rather less favorable crop reports.
Lumber, as for a long time past, continued in
excellent demand. Building activity is wide
read, prices hold all their old advances and
the tendency Is apparently still upward. Re-
fined sugar Is in rather better demand, but
raws are rather quiet at last: week's decline.
: Hides and leather are strong at old quota-
tions, a new feature reported this week being
a good demand for some grades of upper leath
er for export account. Less favorable weather
has tended to check distributive demand at re-
tail, but ln the wholesale lines trade is regard-
ed as very fair and even satisfactory for the
advanced stage of the season.
I Wheat, including flour, shipments for the
week aggregate 2.212,206 bushels, against 3/284.
- bushels last week, 4.064,832 bushels In the
corresponding week of 18-8; 2,655,8â bushels In
1897; 1,-06.005 bushels in 1894 and. 2,754,023 bush-
els in 1893. Since July 1 this season the ex-
! ports of wheat aggregate 205,271,C54 bushels,
I against 286,4*7,456 bushels last year. Business
I failures for the week number 166 against 169
last week, 211 in this week a ' year ago, 245
ln 1897, 216 in 1896 and 206 In 1895. â¢
Business failures In Canada number 18,
against 21 last week, 36 in this week a year ago,
21 in 1897. 28 In 1896, and 25 In 1895.
DUN'S REVIEW OF TRADE.
NEW YORK, May ID.â R. G. Dun ft Co.'s
Weekly Review of Trade to-morrow will say:
While buying 200,000 tons of Bessemer pig at
Pittsburg gave reason for the advance in price
to $16 50, with gray forge quoted at *15 per
ton, it is not quite clear as it might be that
the output, which is 255,000 tons monthly, ac-
Iâ¢ cording to the Pittsburg Record, and has since
been largely Increased by the addition of many
furnaces, will continue to fall^below < the de-
mand. For the requirements Tn various de-
partments for manufactured products It must
I be recognized as scarcely measurable, and does
| not seem as much diminished as might be ex-
pected by higher prices and long delay In de-
liver)*- f In plates, for instance, the demand
I covers many, thousand tons for bridges at Chi
THE SAN /. FRANCISCO CALL, SATTJIU) AT, MAY 20. 15 99. â 
cago and Pittsburg and a Philadelphia concern
accepted several thousand tons and ' afterward
was obliged to refuse more after a concern In
the same city refusing over 1200 tons. The j
shipments of boots and shoes from the East
have been for, two weeks of May 22 per cent I
larger than last year and 50.9 per cent larger j
than ln 1892. Better prices, which have been .
asked for several months, are now more gen- ;
erally paid for men's w ar, though most works
are covered by earlier orders for four months i
ahead or more. Leather shows hesitation, deal-
ers apparently questioning whether the rise
has not been too much. At Chicago the same
question runs through the hide market, though
advances are slight.
Speculators are doing a large business ln i
wool, acme foreign and some American. They j
have bought 7,000,000 pounds of Australian wool j
ln bond here, as Is now reported, andl, about j
1,000,000 rounds of domestic Territory wool is j
also said to have been sold for export at about '
42c clean. But this does not cast much light ,
on the - question whether speculative eales I
abroad have yet been covered or will be within >
a short time, nor Is there any indication that I
the market here will respond to the temporary I
foreign demand. The orders for woolen goods j
are decidedly better and although the Woolen j
Association is not yet in operation It seems to j
have given some confidence to speculators In !
woolen goods. ';â -".. v'
Wheat has advanced during the past week '
about 4c and nobody can tell why. although j
the exports in the past two weeks have been .
1,569,006 bushels, flour included, against 8,135,
- last year. Better shipments were expected i
after the opening of navigation and there, is j
really no use in considering the dreams of j
Western speculators and crop reports at this
time. No one can form a fair judgment at .the
middle of May regarding the output which will
be visible to everybody ln the middle of July. |
The best that can be done Is to accept the crop
reports, official and other, almost within defi
nite allowances. Better shipments are ex-
pected after the opening of navigation and
are in fact coming, the outward movement
from Duluth alone in two weeks reaching
nearly 4,000,000 bushels. Lack of rain in Cali
fornia has caused a large reduction ln all es
timates for that State and Pacific exports are
decreasing, and ' holders are less ready to ac-
cept current prices with the prospect of. a
shortage. i ,
Failures for the week have been 147 in the
United States, against. 250 last year, and 17
in Canada, against 29 last year.
